    Now evaluate $g$ at $1/a$:
    \[
        \begin{aligned}
            g\!\left(\frac{1}{a}\right)
    &= \left(\frac{1}{a}\right)^n f(a) \\
    &= \frac{f(a)}{a^n} \\
    &= 0 .
        \end{aligned}
    \]
    Hence $1/a$ is a root of $g$ whenever $a \neq 0$ is a root of $f$.
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\section{Homomorphisms of rings, Prime/maximal ideals}
\begin{defn}
    A \textbf{ring homomorphism} 
    \[
        \phi : R \to S
    \]
    between rings satisfies 
    \begin{align*}
        \phi(a+b) &= \phi(a) + \phi(b) \\ 
        \phi(ab) &= \phi(a)\phi(b)
    \end{align*}
    and typically 
    \[
        \phi(1_R) = 1_S
    \]
\end{defn}

\begin{thm}
    Let $R,S$ be rings and $\phi : R \to S$ a homomorphism. 
    Then $\ker \phi$ is an ideal of $R$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
    Let $a,b \in \ker \phi$.

    \[
        \phi(a-b) = 0
    \]
    so closed under subtraction. 
    For any $r\in R$ we have 
    \[
        \phi(ra) = \phi(r)0 = 0
    \]
    so $ra \in \ker \phi$.

    Therefore kernels behave like normal subgroups.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}[First Isomorphism Theorem for Rings]
    If 
    \[
        \phi : R \to S
    \]
    then 
    \[
        R/\ker \phi \cong \operatorname{Im} \phi
    \]
    meaning every homomorphic image of a ring is a quotient 
    by an ideal.
\end{thm}
\begin{ex}
    Take 
    \[
        \phi : \mathbb Z \to \mathbb Z_n
    \]
    as 
    \[
        \phi(k) = k \mod n
    \]
    The kernel is then 
    \[
        \ker \phi = n\mathbb Z
    \]
    so 
    \[
        \mathbb Z/n\mathbb Z \cong \mathbb Z_n
    \]
\end{ex}
\begin{defn}
    If $I$ is an ideal of a ring $R$ we define 
    \[
        R/I
    \]
    just like quotient groups, with operations 
    \begin{align*}
        (a+I) + (b + I) &= (a+b) + I \\ 
        (a+I)(b+I) &= ab + I 
    \end{align*}
\end{defn}

\subsection{Prime Ideals}
\begin{defn}
    An ideal $P \subset R$ is \textbf{prime} if 
    \[
        ab \in P \Rightarrow a \in P \ \text{or} \ b \in P
    \]
\end{defn}
\begin{ex}
    In $\mathbb Z$ all ideals are $(n)$ and the prime ideals are 
    $(p)$ where $p$ is a prime number. 
    If $a,b\in (p)$ then $p | ab$ so $p | a$ or $p | b$.
\end{ex}
\begin{thm}
    Suppose $P \subset R$ is an ideal.
    Then 
    \[
        P \ \text{prime} \Leftrightarrow R/P \ \text{is an integral 
        domain}
    \]
\end{thm}

\subsection{Maximal Ideals}
\begin{defn}
    An ideal $M \subset R$ is \textbf{maximal} if 
    $M \neq R$ and there is no ideal strictily between $M$ and 
    $R$, meaning 
    \[
        M \subset I \subset R
    \]
    implies $M = I$ or $R = I$.
\end{defn}
\begin{thm}
    Let $M \subset R$ be an ideal. 

    Then 
    \[
        M \ \text{maximal} \Leftrightarrow R/M \ \text{is a field}
    \]
\end{thm}

An important note is that maximal $\Rightarrow$ prime, but not 
necessarily the other way around, so being maximal is a strictly 
stronger condition. This is easy to see since a field 
is also an integral domain, but not vice versa.

\newpage 
\section{Problems problems and more problems}
\begin{prob}[Problem 26.12 Fraleigh's p.244]
    Give an example to show that a factor ring of an integral domain 
    may be a field.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    Recalling that an integral domain is a nonzero, commutative, 
    unital ring, we give the following example.

    Consider the integral domain $\mathbb Z$.
    Take some prime $p \in \mathbb Z$ and examine the factor ring 
    $\mathbb Z/\langle p\rangle$. It is known that 
    \[
        \mathbb Z/\langle p\rangle \cong \mathbb Z_p
    \]
    The integers modulo a prime is a field.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 26.13 Fraleigh's p.244]
    Give an example to show that a factor ring of an integral domain 
    may have $0$ divisors.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    Consider again the integers $\mathbb Z$. Take the ideal 
    generated by $6$ 
    \[
        \langle 6 \rangle = \{\ldots,-6,0,6,\ldots\}
    \]
    Notice that 
    \[
        \mathbb Z/\langle 6 \rangle \cong \mathbb Z_6
    \]
    and $2,3 \in \mathbb Z_6$, but 
    \[
        2\cdot 3 \equiv 0
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 26.25 Fraleigh's p.244]
    Show that if $R$ is a ring with unity and $N$ is an ideal 
    of $R$ such that $N \neq R$, then $R/N$ is a ring with unity.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Let $R$ be a unital ring with ideal $N \neq R$.

    We study the quotient ring 
    \[
        R / N \neq \{N\}
    \]
    Let $(a + N) \in R / N$. Consider $(1+N) \in R/N$ 
    Then 
    \[
        (a+N)(1 + N) = (a\cdot1) + N = (1 \cdot a) + N 
        = (1 + N)(a + N)
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 26.26 Fraleigh's p.245]
    Let $R$ be a commutative ring and let $a \in R$. 
    Show that $I_a = \{x \in R \bigm| ax = 0\}$ is an ideal of 
    $R$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We consider, for some arbitrary element of a commutative ring $R$,
    the set of right (and correspondingly left) zero divisors of 
    $a$ 
    \[
        I_a = \{x \in R \bigm| ax = 0\}
    \]
    We know $I_a \neq \emptyset$ as $a\cdot 0 = 0$. 
    Let $b,c \in I_a$. Then $ab = ac = 0$, so
    \[
        a(b-c) = ab - ac = 0 - 0 = 0
    \]
    so $b-c \in I_a$. Therefore $(I_a,+) \leq (R,+)$. Let 
    $r \in R$. As stated $ab = 0$ and furthermore 
    \[
        a(rb) = a(br) = (ab)r = 0r = 0
    \]
    so $rb \in I_a$. Thus $I_a$ is an ideal of $R$.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 26.30 Fraleigh's p.245]
    An element $a$ of a ring $R$ is nilpotent if $a^n = 0$ for some 
    $n \in \mathbb Z^+$. Show that the collection of all nilpotent 
    elements in a commutative ring $R$ is an ideal, the nilradical 
    of $R$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Let $R$ be a commutative ring and let $A \subseteq R$ be the 
    set 
    \[
        A = \{a \in R \bigm| \exists n \in \mathbb Z^+ : a^n = 0\}
    \]
    $A \neq \emptyset$ as $0^1 = 0$. Let $a,b \in A$. Then there 
    is some $m,n \in \mathbb Z^+$ such that 
    \[
        a^m = 0 \ \text{and} \ b^n = 0
    \]
    Let $k = m + n \in \mathbb Z^+$. 
    \begin{align*}
        (a-b)^k 
        &= \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{k}\binom{k}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i}\\
        &= \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{m+n}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i}\\
        &= \displaystyle\sum_{i=m+1}^{m+n}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i}
        \\
        &+ \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{m}\binom{m+n}{i}a^{i}(-b)^{k-i}
    \end{align*}
    Notice that $\sum_{i=m+1}^{m+n}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i}$ has 
    $i \geq m + 1$ for every term so $a^i = 0$ for all terms. 
    Hence every term is $0$. 
    For $\sum_{i=0}^{m}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i}$ we have 
    $k-i \geq n$ so $(-b)^{k-i} = 0$ for every term. 
    Therefore 
    \[
        \displaystyle\sum_{i=m+1}^{m+n}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i} 
        + \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{m}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i} 
        = 0 + 0 = 0
    \]
    Therefore $(a-b) \in A$. 
    Now let $r \in R$ and observe that 
    \[
        (ra)^m = r^ma^m = r^m \cdot 0 = 0 
    \]
    so $ra \in A$. Thus $A$ constitutes an ideal of $R$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 26.34 Fraleigh's p.245]
    Let $R$ be a commutative ring and $N$ an ideal of $R$. 
    Show that the set $\sqrt N$ of all $a \in R$, such that 
    $a^n \in N$ for some $n \geq \mathbb Z^+$, is an ideal of 
    $R$, the \textbf{radical ideal} of $N$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Let $N$ be an ideal of some commutative ring $R$. 

    Let 
    \[
    \sqrt N = \left\{ a \in R \bigm| \exists n \in \mathbb Z^+ 
    \ \text{s.t.} \ a^n \in N\right\}
    \]
    First, observe that $\sqrt N \neq \emptyset$ as 
    $0 = 0^1 \in N$. Next, let $a,b \in \sqrt N$. We 
    show that $(a-b)^k \in N$ for some $k$, i.e. $a-b \in \sqrt N$. 
    By assumption there exists $m,n \in \mathbb Z^+$ so that 
    $a^m,b^n \in N$.  
    Let $k = m+n$. Then 
    \begin{align*}
        (a-b)^k 
        &= \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{k}\binom{k}{i}a^i(-b)^{k-i} \\ 
        &= \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{m+n}
        \binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{m+n-i}\\
        &=\displaystyle\sum_{i=m+1}^{m+n}
        \binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{m+n-i}\\
        &+ \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{m}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{m+n-i}
    \end{align*}
    The sum $\sum_{i=m+1}^{m+n}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{m+n-i}$ has 
    $i \geq m+1$ so each term is of the form $a^m(a \cdots a) 
    (-b)^{m+n-i}$. By assumption $a^m \in N$ and as 
    $R$ is commutative we can rearange terms and see that 
    since $N$ is an ideal of $R$ and $a\cdots a(-b)^{m+n-i} \in R$ 
    we have that each term is in $N$ by the multiplication property 
    of the ideal. As $N$ is a group under the addition, the sum 
    of these terms is in $N$. Apply this same argument to the 
    sum from $i=0$ to $m$, observing that $m+n - i \geq n$, to 
    find that $\sum_{i=0}^{m}\binom{m+n}{i}a^i(-b)^{m+n-i} \in N$. 
    Both sums are in $N$ so 
    \[
        (a-b)^k \in N
    \]
    i.e. $a-b \in \sqrt N$. 

    Lastly let $r \in R$ and see that 
    \[
        (ra)^m = r^ma^m \in N 
    \]
    as $N$ is an ideal. Thus $ra \in \sqrt N$.

    Therefore $\sqrt N$ is an ideal of $R$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 27.5 Fraleigh's p.252]
    Find all $c \in \mathbb Z_3$ such that 
    \[
        \mathbb Z_3[x]/\langle x^2 + c \rangle 
    \]
    is a field.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Since $\mathbb Z_3$ is a field, the quotient
    \[
        \mathbb Z_3[x]/\langle x^2+c\rangle
    \]
    is a field if and only if $x^2+c$ is irreducible in 
    $\mathbb Z_3[x]$.

    The elements of $\mathbb Z_3$ are $0,1,2$. We check each case.

    If $c=0$, then $x^2$ is reducible since $x^2=x\cdot x$.

    If $c=1$, we check whether $x^2+1$ has a root in $\mathbb Z_3$.
    The squares in $\mathbb Z_3$ are
    \[
        0^2=0,\quad 1^2=1,\quad 2^2=1.
    \]
    Thus $x^2$ never equals $2$, so $x^2+1$ has no root and is 
    irreducible.

    If $c=2$, then
    \[
        x^2+2=x^2-1=(x-1)(x+1),
    \]
    so it is reducible.

    Therefore the quotient ring is a field only when $c=1$.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 27.15/16 Fraleigh's p.253]
    Find a maximal ideal of $\mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$.

    Find a prime ideal of $\mathbb Z^2$ which is not maximal.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    We want to find the the proper ideal $M \subseteq \mathbb Z 
    \times \mathbb Z$ such that for any ideal $I$, 
    if $M \subseteq I \subseteq \mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$ 
    then $I = \mathbb Z \times 
    \mathbb Z$ or $I = M$.

    The maximal ideals of $\mathbb Z^2$ is 
    $\langle p\rangle \times \mathbb Z$ and 
    $\mathbb Z \times \langle p\rangle$ where 
    $p$ is some prime. $\langle p \rangle^2$ does not work as the 
    quotient would be $\mathbb Z_p^2$. Both factors are nontrivial 
    so this is not a field. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 18 Fraleigh's p.253]
    Is $\mathbb Q[x]/\langle x^2 - 5x + 6\rangle$ a field? Why?
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Recall that the ideal generated by $x^2 - 5x + 6$ is 
    \[
        \langle x^2 -5x + 6\rangle = \{f(x) (x^2 - 5x + 6) : 
        f(x) \in \mathbb Q[x]\}
    \]
    We apply a theorem 
    \[
        \mathbb Q/\langle p \rangle \ \text{is a field} 
        \Leftrightarrow
        p \ \text{is irreducible over} \ \mathbb Q
    \]
    Notice that $x^2 - 5x + 6 = (x-2)(x-3)$ and 
    $x-2,x-3 \in \mathbb Q[x]$, so 
    \[
        \mathbb Q[x]/\langle x^2 - 5x + 6 \rangle
    \]
    is \textbf{not} a field.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 19 Fraleigh's p.253]
    Is $\mathbb Q[x]/\langle x^2 - 6x + 6\rangle$ a field? Why?
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Notice that 
    \[
        x^2 - 6x + 6 = (x-(3-\sqrt 3))(x-(3+\sqrt 3))
    \]
    Notice that $\sqrt 3 \not\in \mathbb Q$, i.e. irreducible since 
    the polynomial is a quadratic, 
    so this quotient ring 
    is a field. ($\cong \mathbb Q(\sqrt 3)$)
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\section{Tackling some extension field problems early}
\begin{prob}[Problem 29.1/3/4 Fraleigh's p.272]
    For each following $\alpha$, show that the given number 
    $\alpha \in \mathbb C$ is algebraic over $\mathbb Q$ by finding 
    $f(x) \in \mathbb Q$ such that $f(\alpha) = 0$. 

    \[
        \alpha = 1+\sqrt 2
    \]
    \[
        \alpha = 1+i
    \]
    \[
        \alpha = \sqrt{1+\sqrt[3]{2}}
    \]
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solutions]
    We begin by considering $\alpha = 1+\sqrt 2$. 
    Let $x = 1+\sqrt 2$. Then 
    \begin{align*}
        x &= 1+\sqrt 2 \\
        \sqrt 2 &= x - 1 \\ 
        2 &= (x-1)^2 \\ 
        x^2 -2x + 1 &= 2 \\ 
        x^2 -2x - 1 &= 0
    \end{align*}
    Thus $x^2 - 2x - 1 \in \mathbb Q$ is zero for 
    $x = \alpha = 1 + \sqrt 2$, hence $1 + \sqrt 2$ is algebraic over
    $\mathbb Q$.

    Next we look at $\alpha = 1+i$.
    Set $x = 1+i$.
    \begin{align*}
        i &= x - 1 \\ 
        -1 &= (x-1)^2 \\ 
        -1 &= x^2 - 2x + 1 \\
        x^2 -2x +2 &= 0
    \end{align*}
    $f(x) = x^2 - 2x + 2 \in \mathbb Q[x]$ and 
    $f(\alpha) = 0$ so $\alpha = 1 + i$ is algebraic over 
    $\mathbb Q$.

    Lastly let $\alpha = \sqrt{1+\sqrt[3]{2}}$.
    \begin{align*}
        x &= \sqrt{1+\sqrt[3]{2}} \\ 
        x^2 &= 1 + 2^{1/3} \\ 
        x^2 - 1 &= 2^{1/3} \\ 
        (x^2-1)^3 &= 2 \\ 
        (x^2-1)^3 - 2 &= 0
    \end{align*}
    As above ... $\alpha$ is algebraic over $\mathbb Q$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 29.18 Fraleigh's p.272]
    a. Show that $x^2 + 1$ is irreducible in $\mathbb Z_3[x]$.

    b. Let $\alpha$ be a zero of $x^2 + 1$ in an extension field of 
    $\mathbb Z_3$. As in Example 29.19, give the multiplication and 
    additions tables for the nine elements of $\mathbb Z_3[\alpha]$, 
    written in the order $0,1,2,\alpha,2\alpha,3\alpha, 1 + \alpha, 
    1 + 2\alpha, 2 + \alpha$ and $2 + 2 \alpha$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Let $f(x) = x^2 + 1$. $f(x)$ is a classical introductory example 
    to motivate the complex numbers, having 
    $\alpha = \pm i \in \mathbb C\setminus{\mathbb R}$ as zero.  

    As $f$ is a degree $2$ polynomial with no zeros in $\mathbb Z_3$ 
    we conclude that it is irreducible in $\mathbb Z_3[x]$.
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\section{Trying an earlier exam}
\begin{prob}[Problem 1a, Exam 2023]
    Prove that the set 
    \[
        S = \{2^n \bigm| n \in \mathbb Z\}
    \]
    equipped with the usual operation of multiplication is a group.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    It suffices to show that $(S,\cdot)$ is isomorphic to a known 
    group. Let $\phi : \mathbb Z \to S$ be defined by 
    \[
        \phi(n) = 2^n
    \]
    It is clear that $\phi$ is a bijection as the preimage of 
    $2^n$ is $n$ and $2^n = 2^m$ iff $n = m$. 

    Lastly we show $\phi$ to be a homomorphism. 
    \begin{align*}
        \phi(n+m) &= 2^{n+m} \\ 
                  &= 2^n \cdot 2^m \\ 
                  &= \phi(n)\phi(m)
    \end{align*}
    Thus $(S,\cdot)$ is isomorphic to $(\mathbb Z, +)$ 
    which is a known group.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 1b, Exam 2023]
    List all finite abelian groups of order $20$, up to isomorphism.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    The finite abelian groups of order $20$ are trivially 
    finitely generated so the fundamental theorem of finitely 
    generated abelian groups applies. 

    I.e. 
    \[
        G \cong \left(\prod_{i=1}^n 
        \mathbb Z_{p_i^{e_i}}\right) \times 
        \mathbb Z^k
    \]
    We are asked to find those of order $20$ (finite) so 
    \[
        G \cong \prod_{i=1}^n \mathbb Z_{p_i^{e_i}}
    \]
    where $\prod_{i=1}^n p_i^{e_i} = 20$. 

    $20 = 4\cdot5 = 2\cdot2\cdot 5$. Thus there exists exactly 
    two abelian groups of order $20$, namely 
    \[
        \mathbb Z_4 \times \mathbb Z_5
    \]
    and
    \[
        \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_5
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 1c, Exam 2023]
    Let $A_4$ be the alternating group on $4$ letters, that is 
    the subgroup of $S_4$ consisting of even permutations. 
    Find elements $\sigma_1,\sigma_2,\sigma_3 \in A_4$ such that 
    $\sigma_i$ has order $i$ (for $i = 1,2,3$). 
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    $\sigma_1$ is the trivial permutation, i.e. the one which sends 
    \[
        1 \mapsto 1, 2 \mapsto 2, \ \text{etc.}
    \]
    Next a candidate for $\sigma_2$ is any permutation which is 
    the composition of two transpositions, e.g. 
    \[
        (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4)
    \]
    Notice that 
    \[
        (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4) \circ (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4) 
    \]
    sends 
    \[
        1 \to 2 \to 1, 2 \to 1 \to 2, 3 \to 4 \to 3, 4 \to 3 \to 4
    \]
    So $(1 \ 2)(3 \ 4)$ has order $2$. 

    An exampele of $\sigma_3$ is something like  
    \[
        (1 \ 2 \ 3) = (1 \ 3)(1 \ 2)
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 1d, Exam 2023]
    Prove that $A_4$ is not abelian.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Observe that $(1 \ 2)(3 \ 4)$ and $(1 \ 3)(1 \ 2)$ do not 
    commute.

    \[
        (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4) \circ (1 \ 3)(1 \ 2)  
    \]
    sends 
    \begin{align*}
        1 \mapsto 2 \mapsto 1 \\ 
        2 \mapsto 3 \mapsto 4 \\ 
        3 \mapsto 1 \mapsto 2 \\ 
        4 \mapsto 4 \mapsto 3
    \end{align*}
    So 
    \[
        (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4) \circ (1 \ 3)(1 \ 2)  
        = (2 \ 4 \ 3)
    \]
    while 
    \[
        (1 \ 3)(1 \ 2) \circ (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4)
    \]
    sends 
    \begin{align*}
        3 \mapsto 4 \mapsto 4 \\
    \end{align*}
    So we can already see that 
    \[
        (1 \ 3)(1 \ 2) \circ (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4) 
        \neq 
        (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4) \circ (1 \ 3)(1 \ 2)
    \]
    Hence $A_4$ is not abelian.
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\begin{prob}[Problem 2a, Exam 2023]
    Determine the $0$-divisors in the ring $\mathbb Z_{10}$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    $\mathbb Z_{10}$ is commutative so left- and right 
    divisors coincide. 

    \begin{align*}
        (2)(5) &= 10 \equiv 0 \\ 
        (4)(5) &= 20 \equiv 0 \\ 
        (6)(5) &= 30 \equiv 0 \\ 
        (8)(5) &= 40 \equiv 0 \\ 
    \end{align*}
    so the $0$-divisrors in $\mathbb Z_{10}$ are 
    $5$ with any power of $2$, which just so happens to be 
    the nonzero elements of $\mathbb Z_{10}$ which are not coprime to 
    $\mathbb Z_{10}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 2b, Exam 2023]
    Let $R$ be the ring $\mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$, where 
    addition and multiplication is given by, for $(a,b),(c,d)\in 
    \mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$, setting 
    \begin{align*}
        (a,b)+(c,d)&=(a+c,b+d) \\ 
        (a,b)(c,d)&=(ac,bd)
    \end{align*}
    Let 
    \[
        I = \{(a,0) | a \in \mathbb Z\}\subset R
    \]
    Prove that $I$ is an ideal of $R$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We show that $I$ is a subgroup of $(R,+)$ with the 
    proper behavior w.r.t left-multiplication.

    First we begin by observing that 
    $(0,0) \in I \neq \emptyset$. 
    Let $(a,0),(b,0) \in I$. 
    Then we see that 
    \begin{align*}
        (a,0)-(b,0) &= (a,0)+(-b,0) \\ 
                    &= (a-b, 0 + 0) \\ 
                    &= (a-b,0) \in I
    \end{align*}
    So $I$ is a subgroup of the additive group of $R$. 

    Lastly let $(c,d) \in R$. 
    Then 
    \begin{align*}
        (c,d)(a,0) &= (ca,d0) \\ 
                   &= (ca,0) \in I
    \end{align*}
    Thus $I$ is an ideal of $R$.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 2c, Exam 2023]
    Prove that $I$ is a prime ideal and that $I$ is not 
    a maximal ideal. 
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    It is clear that $I \neq R$ as $(1,1) \in R \setminus I$. 

    Now suppose $(ab,0) \in I$. 
    Then 
    \begin{align*}
        (ab,0) &= (a,c)(b,d) 
    \end{align*}
    with $a,b,c,d \in \mathbb Z$ and $cd = 0$. 
    In $\mathbb Z$ there are no zero-divisors, thus 
    \[
        c = 0 \ \text{or} \ d = 0
    \]
    and hence $(a,c) \in I$ or $(b,d) \in I$.

    We show that $I$ is not maximal by giving some ideal $J$ so that 
    \[
        I \subseteq J \subseteq R
    \]
    with $I \neq J$ and $J \neq R$.

    Let $J$ be the set of $(a,b) \in R$ so that 
    $b$ is even. Formally 
    \[
        J = \{(a,b) \in R \bigm| b = 2n \ \text{for some} \ n 
        \in \mathbb Z\}
    \]
    It is trival to check that this is an ideal and 
    that 
    \[
        I \subsetneq J \subsetneq R
    \]
\end{proof}

\newpage
\begin{prob}[Problem 3a, Exam 2023]
    Let $p$ be a prime number, and let 
    $f(x) = x^p - x \in \mathbb Z_p[x]$.
    Determine (with justification) the zeros of 
    $f(x)$ in $\mathbb Z_p[x]$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    The zeros are all numbers in $\mathbb Z_p$. 
    We can apply Fermat's little theorem 
    \[
        a^p \equiv a \mod p
    \]
    to get that 
    \[
        f(x) = x^p - x^p = x-x = 0
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 3b, Exam 2023]
    Consider $x^2 + 1 \in \mathbb Z_3[x]$. Prove that the ring 
    $\mathbb Z_3[x]/\langle x^2 + 1\rangle$ is a field, and 
    determine its number of elements. 
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We use the following theorem: 
    
    For some field $F$ and $f \in F[x]$ 
    \[
        F[x]/\langle f(x) \rangle
    \] 
    is a field if and only if $f(x)$ is irreducible in $F[x]$. 
    Since $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ is a degree $2$ polynomial we see 
    that $f$ is irreducible in $\mathbb Z_3[x]$ if and only if 
    $f$ has no zeros in $\mathbb Z_3[x]$. 

    It is known that $f(x)$ is irreducible since it is the motivating
    introductory example for the complex numbers with solution 
    $x = \pm i$. 

    We quickly check that $f(0) \neq 0$, $f(1) \neq 0$ and 
    $f(2) \neq 0$ to confirm this. 

    As $3$ is prime we see that $\mathbb Z_3$ is a field. 

    We conclude therefore that 
    \[
        \mathbb Z_3[x]/\langle x^2 + 1\rangle
    \]
    is a field.
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\section{Extension fields \& Vector spaces}
\begin{defn}
    A \textbf{field extension} of a field $F$ is a field 
    $K$ with $F \subseteq K$.

    It is common to write 
    \[
        K / F
    \]
\end{defn}
Some basic examples of the above include $\mathbb Q \subseteq 
\mathbb R$, $\mathbb Q \subseteq \mathbb C$. 

As we have seen earlier, 
\[
    \mathbb Z_3 \subseteq \mathbb Z_3[x]/\langle x^2 + 1\rangle 
    \cong \mathbb Z_3(i)
\]

\begin{defn}
    Given a field $F$ and an element $\alpha \not\in F$, define 
    \[
        F(\alpha)
    \]
    to be the smallest field containing both $F$ and $\alpha$.
\end{defn}
\begin{thm}
    If $\alpha$ satisfies a polynomial over $F$, then 
    \[
        F(\alpha) \cong F[x]/\langle f(x) \rangle
    \]
    where $f$ is the minimal polynomial.

    In other words, if $\alpha$ is algebraic over $F$ then this 
    holds.
\end{thm}
\begin{defn}
    The minimal polynomial of $\alpha$ over $F$ is  
    the monic polynomial of smallest degree in $F[x]$ such that 
    $\alpha$ is a zero of said polynomial.
\end{defn}
\begin{defn}
    The degree of an extension $[K : F]$ is the dimension of 
    $K$ as a vector space over $F$.
\end{defn}
\begin{ex}
    $[\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2) : \mathbb Q] = 2$ with basis 
    $\{1, \sqrt2\}$.
\end{ex}
\begin{thm}
    If $K/F$ is a field extension then $K$ is a vector space over 
    $F$.
\end{thm}
\begin{thm}
    If minimal polynomial has degree $n$ then 
    \[
        [F(\alpha) : F] = n
    \]
\end{thm}
\begin{thm}[Tower Law]
    If 
    \[
        F \subseteq K \subseteq L
    \]
    then 
    \[
        [L : F] = [L : K][K : F]
    \]
\end{thm}

\newpage 
\begin{prob}[Problem 4a, Exam 2023]
    Let $f(x) = x^5 - 8x^4 + 2x + 2 \in \mathbb Q[x]$. You may use 
    w/o proof that $f$ is irreducible. 

    Let $\alpha \in \overline{\mathbb Q}$ be a zero of 
    $f$. Compute $[\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q]$, and give 
    a basis for $\mathbb Q(\alpha)$ as a vector space over 
    $\mathbb Q$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Since $f(x) \in \mathbb{Q}[x]$ is irreducible and $\alpha$ 
    is a root,
    $f$ is the minimal polynomial of $\alpha$ over $\mathbb{Q}$.

    Therefore,
    \[
        [\mathbb{Q}(\alpha) : \mathbb{Q}] = \deg f = 5.
    \]

    A basis for $\mathbb{Q}(\alpha)$ over $\mathbb{Q}$ is
    \[
        \{1,\alpha,\alpha^2,\alpha^3,\alpha^4\}.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 4b, Exam 2023]
    Let $\beta \in \mathbb Q(\alpha) \setminus \mathbb Q$. 
    Prove that $\mathbb Q(\beta) = \mathbb Q(\alpha)$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    $\beta \in \mathbb Q(\alpha)$ and $\beta \not \in \mathbb Q$, 
    by assumption. So 
    \[
        \mathbb Q \subseteq \mathbb Q(\beta) \subseteq 
        \mathbb Q(\alpha)
    \]
    We know that 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q] = 
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q(\beta)] \cdot 
        [\mathbb Q(\beta) : \mathbb Q]
    \]
    $5$ is prime so one of the factors is $5$ and the other is $1$. 
    It can't be the case that 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q(\beta)] = 5
    \]
    since that would imply $\mathbb Q(\beta) = \mathbb Q$ which 
    is not the case, by assumption. Hence 
    $\mathbb Q(\alpha) = \mathbb Q(\beta)$.
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\section{Some problems relevant to Fraleigh's sections 29-33}
\begin{prob}
    Let $\alpha = \sqrt 2 + \sqrt 5$. Find the minimal 
    polynomial for $\alpha$ over $\mathbb Q$.
    Then compute degree and identify a basis.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    \begin{align*}
        x &= \sqrt 2 + \sqrt 5 \\ 
        x^2 &= 7 + 2\sqrt{10} \\ 
        x^2 - 7 &= 2\sqrt{10} \\ 
        \frac{x^2-7}{2} &= \sqrt{10} \\ 
        \frac{(x^2-7)(x^2-7)}{4} &= 10 \\ 
        \frac{x^4 -14x^2 + 49 - 40}{4} &= 0 \\ 
        \frac{x^4 - 14x^2 + 9}{4} &= 0 \\ 
        x^4 - 14x^2 + 9 &= 0 \\ 
    \end{align*}
    is the minimal polynomial of $\alpha$. 
    It is degree $r$ and thus a basis of 
    $Q(\alpha)$ over $\mathbb Q$ is 
    $\{1, \alpha, \alpha^2, \alpha^3\}$.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Give an example of a field extension which is finitely generated 
    and not finite.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    Consider $\mathbb C/\mathbb R$. $\mathbb C$ is a 
    $2$-dimensional vector space over $\mathbb R$, but 
    as a field $|\mathbb C| = \aleph_1$ and 
    \[
        |\mathbb C\setminus \mathbb R| = \aleph_1
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Let $\alpha, \beta$ be algebraic over $F$. 
    Prove 
    \[
        [F(\alpha,\beta):F] \leq [F(\alpha):F][F(\beta):F]
    \]
    and explain when equality is achieved.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Suppose $\alpha,\beta$ algebraic over some field $F$.
    Then there exists minimal polynomials 
    \[
        f,g \in F[x]
    \]
    so that $f(\alpha) = 0$ and $g(\beta) = 0$. 
    With 
    \[
        F(\alpha) = 
        \left\{
        \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{\deg f - 1}c_i\alpha_i \biggm|
        c_i \in F
        \right\}
    \]
    and 
    \[
        F(\beta) = 
        \left\{
        \displaystyle\sum_{i=0}^{\deg g - 1}c_i\beta_i \biggm|
        c_i \in F
        \right\}
    \]
    Then 
    \[
        [F(\alpha):F][F(\beta):F] = (\deg f-1)(\deg g-1)
    \]
    It is clear that the two expressions are equal when 
    $g$ is irreducible in $F(\alpha)$, recalling 
    \[
        F \subseteq F(\alpha) \subseteq F(\alpha,\beta)
    \]
    A nice example is $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 5)$.

    $F(\alpha,\beta)$ is an extension field of $F(\alpha)$ so if 
    $\beta$ and $\alpha$ are independent in their minimal polynomials 
    then we get equality. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 31.3/5/7 Fraleigh's p.291]
    Find the degree and basis for the given field extensions. 
    Be prepared to justify your answers. 

    3. $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt{18})$ over $\mathbb Q$ 

    5. $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt[3]2)$ over $\mathbb Q$ 

    7. $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)$ over $\mathbb Q$
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    Notice that 
    \[
        \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt{18}) 
        = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)
    \]
    since $\sqrt{18} = \sqrt{9}\sqrt 2 = 3 \sqrt 2 \in \mathbb 
    Q(\sqrt 2)$.
    Now notice that 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2) : \mathbb Q] = 2 
    \]
    since $x^2 - 2$ is irreducible over $\mathbb Q$.
    Lastly we conclude that $\sqrt 3 \not \in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$ 
    since 
    \begin{align*}
        \sqrt 3 &= a + b\sqrt 2 \\ 
        3 &= a^2 + 2ab\sqrt 2 + 2b^2
    \end{align*}
    Thus $2ab = 0$ so $a = 0$ or $b = 0$. Both cases work out 
    to show that $a,b \not\in \mathbb Q$. Therefore 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3) : \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)] = 2
    \]
    By the tower law: 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3) : \mathbb Q] = 4
    \]
    with a standard basis being 
    $\{1,\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt 6\}$.

    Next up we examine $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt[3] 2)$.

    \begin{align*}
        \sqrt[3]2 &= a + b\sqrt 2 \\ 
        2 &= (a+b\sqrt 2)(a^2 + 2b^2 + 2ab\sqrt 2) \\ 
        2 &= a^3 + 2ab^2 + 2a^2b\sqrt 2 + a^2b\sqrt 2 + 
        2b^3 \sqrt 2 + 2ab^2
    \end{align*}
    The left side is rational so we require $b = 0$, but then 
    $a = \sqrt[3] 2 \not\in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$. Hence we conclude 
    that 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt[3]2) : \mathbb Q] = 6
    \]

    For problem 7 notice that we have already shown it for 
    problem 3.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 31.23 Fraleigh's]
    Show that if $E$ is a finite extension of a field 
    $F$ and $[E:F]$ is prime, then $E$ is a simple 
    extension of $F$ and, indeed $E = F(\alpha)$ for 
    every $\alpha \in E\setminus F$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We assume $E$ is a finite extension of $F$ and in particular 
    \[
        [E:F] = p
    \]
    for some prime $p$. 
    Let $\alpha \in E\setminus F$ then $\alpha$ is algebraic 
    over $F$ so $\alpha$ has a minimal polynomial over 
    $F$ and 
    \[
        [F(\alpha) : F] = \deg m_\alpha(x)
    \]
    Now note that 
    \[
        F \subseteq F(\alpha) \subseteq E
    \]
    since $\alpha \in E$. By the tower law: 
    \[
        [E:F] = [E:F(\alpha)][F(\alpha):F] = p
    \]
    so one side of the product must be $1$. Since $\alpha \not\in F$
    it must be the case that 
    \[
        [F(\alpha) : F] > 1
    \]
    so $[F(\alpha):F] = p$, i.e. 
    \[
        E = F(\alpha)
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 31.24 Fraleigh's p.292]
    Prove that $x^2 - 3$ is irreducible over 
    $\mathbb Q(\sqrt[3]2)$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We can make the simple observation that 
    $\sqrt 3 \not\in \mathbb Q(\sqrt[3]2)$.
    Let $\alpha = \sqrt[3]2$. Then 
    \[
        \mathbb Q(\alpha) = \{a+b\alpha + c\alpha^2 : a,b,c \in 
        \mathbb Q\}
    \]
    So if $\sqrt 3 \in \mathbb Q(\alpha)$ then 
    \begin{align*}
        \sqrt 3 &= a + b\alpha + c\alpha^2
    \end{align*}
    Suppose this holds. Since $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 3) \subseteq 
    \mathbb Q(\alpha)$ then by the tower law 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha)] = [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q(\sqrt 3)]
        \cdot [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 3) : \mathbb Q]
    \]
    But then 
    \[
        3 = [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q(\sqrt 3)] \cdot 2
    \]
    but $2$ does not divide $3$ so this is impossible.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 31.29 Fraleigh's p.292]
    Let $E$ be a finite extension of $F$, and let 
    $p(x) \in F[x]$ be irreducible over $F$ and have degree 
    that is not a divisor of $[E:F]$. Show that $p(x)$ has 
    no zeros in $E$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Let $\alpha \in E$ be a hypothetical zero of $p(x)$.
    Since $p$ is irreducible in $F$ and $p(\alpha) = 0$, 
    $p$ must be the minimal polynomial of $\alpha$.

    Thus 
    \[
        [F(\alpha) : F] = \deg p(x) 
    \]
    Using the tower law 
    \[
        [E:F] = [E:F(\alpha)] [F(\alpha) : F]
    \]
    But then $\deg p$ divides $[E:F]$ which, by assumption, 
    is not the case. Hence we have a contradiction.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 31.30 Fraleigh's p.292]
    Let $E$ be an extension of $F$. Let $\alpha \in E$ 
    be algebraic of odd degree over $F$. Show that $\alpha^2$ 
    is algebraic of odd degree over $F$, and $F(\alpha) = 
    F(\alpha^2)$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We fix some $\alpha \in E$ algebraic of odd degree over $F$.
    Then 
    \[
        [F(\alpha) : F] = 2n + 1, \ n \in \mathbb N 
    \]
    Crucially $\alpha \not \in F$, by assumption.
    Now suppose $\alpha^2 \in F$. 
    Then $\alpha^2 \not\in F(\alpha) \setminus F$ so 
    $[F(\alpha):F] = 2$ (since $x^2 - \alpha^2 \in F[x]$ would be a 
    minmal polynomial of $\alpha$), which contradicts our assumption. 
    Thus $\alpha^2 \not\in F$. 
    Now since $\alpha^2 \in F(\alpha)$ we see that 
    $\alpha$ satisfies 
    \[
        x^2 - \alpha^2 \in F(\alpha^2)[x]
    \]
    Hence $[F(\alpha) : F(\alpha^2)] \leq 2$. By the tower law 
    \[
        [F(\alpha) : F] = [F(\alpha) : F(\alpha^2)][F(\alpha^2):F]
    \]
    We see that then $[F(\alpha) : F(\alpha^2)]$ must be $1$, 
    hence 
    \[
        F(\alpha) = F(\alpha^2)
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Let $\alpha = \sqrt 2 + \sqrt 3$.

    Show that $\alpha$ is algebraic over $\mathbb Q$.

    Show that $\mathbb Q(\alpha) = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)$.

    Deduce that $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)$ is a simple extension.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Clearly $\sqrt 2 + \sqrt 3 \not\in \mathbb Q$.
    Now it remains to find a polynomial in $\mathbb Q[x]$ for 
    which $\alpha$ is a zero.
    \begin{align*}
        x &= \sqrt 2 + \sqrt 3 \\ 
        x^2 &= 2 + 2\sqrt 2 \sqrt 3 + 3 \\ 
        x^2 - 5 &= 2 \sqrt 2 \sqrt 3 \\ 
        \frac{x^2 - 5}{2} &= \sqrt 6 \\ 
        \frac{(x^2 - 5)(x^2 - 5)}{4} &= 6 \\ 
        \frac{x^4 - 10x^2 + 25}{4} - 6 &= 0 \\ 
        x^4 - 10x^2 +1 &= 0
    \end{align*}
    and $x^4 - 10x^2 + 1 \in \mathbb Q[x]$ so 
    $\alpha$ is algebraic over $\mathbb Q$.

    Thus 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q] = [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, 
        \sqrt 3): \mathbb Q] = 4
    \]
    The standard basis for $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)$ is 
    $\{1,\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt 2 \sqrt 3\}$ which just so 
    happens to be the basis for $\mathbb Q(\alpha)$.
    We can also take some arbitrary element from either extension 
    field and show how to express it in terms of the other to show 
    that they are indeed equal.

    Since $\mathbb Q(\alpha)$ is simple, it follows from the above 
    observation that 
    \[
        \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3)
    \]
    is indeed simple.
\end{proof}

\section{Some problems from Section 33}
\begin{prob}[Problem 33.4 Fraleigh's]
    Find the number of primitive $8$th roots of unity 
    in $GF(9)$ (also known as $\mathbb F_9$).
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    The multiplicative group 
    $\mathbb F^x_9$ has order $8$ since $0$ is excluded.
    The multiplicative group of a finite field is 
    cyclic so 
    \[
        \mathbb F_9^x \cong \mathcal C_8
    \]
    The $8$th roots of unity are the elements $x \in \mathcal C_8$
    for which 
    \[
        x^8 = 1
    \]
    Since the whole group has order $8$, every nonzero element 
    satisfies this. A primitive $8$th root of unity 
    has order exactly $8$ and the Euler totient function 
    gives 
    \[
        \phi(8) = 4
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 33.9 Fraleigh's p.305]
    Let $\overline{\mathbb Z}_2$ be the algebraic closure 
    of $\mathbb Z_2$, and let 
    $\alpha,\beta \in \overline{\mathbb Z}_2$ be zeros 
    of $x^3 + x^2 + 1$ and of $x^3 + x + 1$, respectively.
    Using the results of this section, show that 
    $\mathbb Z_2(\alpha) = \mathbb Z_2(\beta)$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We begin by showing that the polynomials 
    $f(x) = x^3 + x^2 + 1$ and $g(x) = x^3 + x + 1$ are 
    irreducible over $\mathbb Z_2$.
    These are degree $3$ polynomials and $\mathbb Z_2$ is a field 
    so they are irreducible if and only if 
    they have no zeros in $\mathbb Z_2$.
    \[
        f(0) = 1 \ \text{and} \ f(1) = 1,
    \]
    and 
    \[
        g(0) = 1 \ \text{and} \ g(1) = 1.
    \]
    Thus $f,g$ are irreducible over $\mathbb Z_2$. 
    Hence 
    \[
        [\mathbb Z_2(\alpha) : \mathbb Z_2] 
        = 
        [\mathbb Z_2(\beta) : \mathbb Z_2] 
        = 3
    \]
    So both $\mathbb Z_2(\alpha)$ and $\mathbb Z_2(\beta)$ 
    are field with degree $2^3$. Since $2$ is prime we can 
    use the finite-field theorem and conclude that 
    these two fields are isomorphic. Furthermore 
    in an algebraic closure of some field $\mathbb F_p$, there 
    is exactly one subfield with $p^n$ elements, namely 
    the set of roots of 
    \[
        x^{p^n} - x.
    \]
    Thus 
    \[
        \mathbb Z_2(\alpha) = \mathbb Z_2(\beta).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 33.10 Fraleigh's p.305]
    Show that every irreducible polynomial in 
    $\mathbb Z_p[x]$ is a dvisor of 
    $x^{p^n} - x$ for some $n$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Let $\alpha \in \overline{\mathbb Z}_p$ be the root of 
    some $f(x) \in \mathbb Z_p$, irreducible. 
    Then 
    \[
        [\mathbb Z_p(\alpha) : \mathbb Z_p] = \deg f
    \]
    So $\mathbb Z_p(\alpha)$ is a finite field with 
    $p^m$ elements.
    If a field has $p^m$ elements then 
    $a^{p^m} = a$ for all $a$ in said field.
    Equivalently every elements is a root of 
    \[
        x^{p^m} - x.
    \]
    Then 
    \[
        \alpha^{p^m} - \alpha = 0.
    \]
    If a polynomial $g$ is the minimal polynomial of $\alpha$ 
    over $\mathbb Z_p$, and $\alpha$ is also a root of another 
    polynomial $f$ then 
    \[
        g \bigm| f
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[Problem 3a Exam 2016]
    What is an integral domain? Show that 
    $\mathbb Z[x]/(x^2 + 1)$ is an integral domain. 
    Is $\mathbb Z[x]/(x^2 - 1)$ an integral domain?
    Give reasons for your answer.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution and Proof]
    An integral domain is a non-zero ring with multiplicative 
    identity ($1 \neq 0$) that has no zero divisors.

    For $f(x) \in F[x]$, $F[x]/\langle f(x) \rangle$ is 
    an integral domain iff $f(x)$ is irreducible in 
    $F[x]$.
    Since $x^2 + 1$ is a degree $2$ polynomial it is 
    irreducible if and only if it has no zeros in 
    $\mathbb Z$. $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ is famously a motivating example 
    for complex numbers with zeros $\pm i \in \mathbb C$. 
    A quick sanity check gives $f(0) = 1$, $f(\pm 1) = 2$ 
    and generally $f(n) = f(-n) < f(n + 1) = f(-(n + 1))$ so 
    it ceirtainly does not have any zeros in $\mathbb Z$ (can 
    be proven with induction or standard techniques if necessary).
    Thus $f$ is irreducible and so $\mathbb Z[x]/\langle f(x) 
    \rangle$ is an integral domain.

    Using this same procedure we see that 
    $x^2 - 1$ has zeros $\pm 1 \in \mathbb Z$ and is therefore 
    not irreducible so 
    \[
        \mathbb Z[x]/\langle x^2 - 1 \rangle
    \]
    is not an integral domain.
\end{proof}

\newpage 
\section{Automorphisms of Field Extensions \& 
Isomorphism Extension}
\begin{defn}[Field Automorphism]
    A \textbf{field automorphism} of $E$ is a bijection 
    \[
        \sigma : E \to E
    \]
    such that $\forall x,y \in E$: 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\sigma (x+y) = \sigma(x) + \sigma(y)$ 
        \item $\sigma(xy) = \sigma(x)\sigma(y)$
        \item $\sigma(1) = 1$
    \end{enumerate}
\end{defn}
If $F \subseteq E$ is a field extension we write 
\[
    \operatorname{Aut}(E/F) := 
    \{\sigma : E \to E \bigm| \sigma 
        \ \text{is a field automorphism and} \ 
    \sigma|_F = \operatorname{id}_F\}.
\]

\begin{prop}
    A field automorphism $\sigma$ preserves algebraic 
    structure: 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\sigma(0) = 0$ 
        \item $\sigma(-x) = -\sigma(x)$ 
        \item $\sigma(x^{-1}) = \sigma(x)^{-1}$ for 
            $x \neq 0$
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}[Proof Sketch]
    Straightforward from homomorphism 
    properties. For instance: 
    \[
        1 = \sigma(1) = \sigma(xx^{-1}) 
        = \sigma(x)\sigma(x^{-1})
    \]
    and so on.
\end{proof}

\begin{prop}
    $\operatorname{Aut}(E/F)$ is a group under composition.
\end{prop}

\begin{thm}
    Let $E/F$ be an extension, and let $\alpha \in E$ be 
    algebraic over $F$. Let $m_\alpha(x) \in F[x]$ be a 
    minimal polynomial. 

    Then for any $\sigma \in \operatorname{Aut}(E/F)$, 
    \[
        m_\alpha(\sigma(\alpha)) = 0.
    \]
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
    Write $m_\alpha(x) = \sum_{i=0}^{n}a_ix^i, \ a_i \in F 
    \ (a_n = 1)$. 

    Since $\sigma$ fixes $F$, we have $\sigma(a_i) = a_i$. 
    Then:
    \[
        0 = \sigma(m_\alpha(\alpha)) 
        = \sigma\left(\sum a_i\alpha^i\right) 
        = \sum a_i\sigma(\alpha)^i = m_\alpha(\sigma(\alpha))
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prop}
    Let $E = F(\alpha)$, and let $\sigma \in \operatorname{Aut}(E/F)$.
    Then $\sigma$ is completely determined by $\sigma(\alpha)$.
\end{prop}

\begin{thm}
    Let $E = F(\alpha)$, where $\alpha$ is algebraic over 
    $F$ with minimal polynomial $m_\alpha(x)$. 

    Then 
    \[
        |\operatorname{Aut}(E/F)| \leq \deg m_\alpha.
    \]
\end{thm}
\begin{defn}
    A field embedding 
    \[
        \sigma : F \to E
    \]
    is an injective field homomorphism.
\end{defn}

\begin{thm}
    Let $F \subseteq E = F(\alpha)$, $\sigma : F \to K$ be 
    a field homomorphism, $m_\alpha(x)$ the minimal 
    polynomial of $\alpha$.
    Then there exists an extension 
    \[
        \widetilde\sigma : E \to K
    \]
    with $\widetilde\sigma(\alpha) = \beta$ if and only if 
    $\beta$ is a root of $\sigma(m_\alpha)(x)$.
\end{thm}

\begin{thm}[Isomorphism Extension Theorem]
    Let $\sigma : F \to K$ be a field isomorphism and 
    $\alpha$ algebraic over $F$ with minimal polynomial 
    $m_\alpha(x)$.

    Let $\beta \in \overline K$ be a root of 
    $\sigma(m_\alpha)(x)$. 

    Then $\sigma$ is extends to an isomorphism 
    \[
        \widetilde \sigma : F(\alpha) \to K(\beta)
    \]
    with 
    \[
        \widetilde \sigma(\alpha) = \beta.
    \]
\end{thm}
\begin{thm}[Automorphisms of Finite Fields]
    Let $E = \mathbb F_{p^n}$. Then 
    \[
        \operatorname{Aut}(E/\mathbb F_p) = \langle 
        \phi \rangle
    \]
    where 
    \[
        \phi(x) = x^p
    \]
    and 
    \[
        |\operatorname{Aut}(E/\mathbb F_p)| = n.
    \]
\end{thm}

\section{Exam Practice}
Henceforth these notes will be mostly revision, 
even though I haven't covered all topics yet. 
There will also probably be a lot of problems repeated.

\begin{prob}[1 Exam 2019]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Is $\mathbb Z_{\geq 0}$ equipped with 
            addition a group? Justify your answer.

        \item What are the abelian groups of order $18$ up 
            to isomorphism?

        \item What are the cosets of the subgroup 
            $\langle (0,3)\rangle$ in $\mathbb Z_2 \times 
            \mathbb Z_9$? Describe the quotient 
            \[
                \frac{\mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9}{
                    \langle(0,3)\rangle
                }
            \]
            as a product of finite cyclic groups.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    No it is not a group because there are additive inverses 
    for all elements except $0$. 

    A single counter-example is $1 \in \mathbb Z_{\geq 0}$ 
    which has no inverse.

    \[
        1 + x = 0 \Rightarrow x = -1 \not\in \mathbb Z_{\geq 0}.
    \]
    We can prove it rigorously as follows. 
    $0$ is obviously not the additive inverse of $1$. 
    Suppose $n \in \mathbb Z_{\geq 0}$ is not an inverse 
    of $1$. Assume, for a contradiction, that $n+1$ is 
    an inverse of $1$.
    Then 
    \begin{align*}
        1 + (n + 1) &= 0 \\ 
        n &= -2 
    \end{align*}
    From this we conclude that the inverse of $1$ is the 
    inverse of $2$, i.e 
    \[
        -1 = -2
    \]
    so $1 = 2$. Hence $n+1$ is not an inverse of $1$. 
    By induction no element of $\mathbb Z_{\geq 0}$ is an 
    inverse of $1$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    We use the fundamental theorem of finitely generated 
    abelian groups. $18$ is finite so we can simply find 
    all ways of expressing $18$ as products of prime powers and 
    we'll be done.
    We see that 
    \[
        18 = 2 \cdot 9 = 2 \cdot 3^2 = 2 \cdot 3 \cdot 3
    \]
    so there are two (up to isomprhism) abelian groups of 
    order $18$, namely 
    \[
        \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9 \ \text{and} \ 
        \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3 \times \mathbb Z_3.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    $\langle (0,3) \rangle = \{(0,0), (0,3), (0,6)\}$. 
    The left and right cosets coincide since this group 
    is abelian and they are precisely
    \[
        \{x + y : y \in \langle (0,3)\rangle \}, \ 
        x \in \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9.
    \]
    Since for $(a,b)$, $b$ is taken mod $9$ only $b$ mod 3 
    matters. 
    So the left cosets are 
    \[
        (a,b) + \langle (0,3) \rangle 
    \]
    where $a \in \mathbb Z_2$ and $b \in \mathbb Z_3$.
    Denote $H := \langle (0,3) \rangle$.
    That gives us cosets
    \begin{align*}
        (0,0) + H, \ (0,1) + H, \ (0,2) + H \\ 
        (1,0) + H, \ (1,1) + H, \ (1,2) + H 
    \end{align*}
    i.e. $6$ cosets, which is what we expect 
    because the order of $\mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9$ is 
    $18$ and $18/|H| = 6$. The quotient groups is abelian 
    and $6 = 2 \cdot 3$ so necessarily 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9}{\langle (0,3)\rangle} 
        \cong \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3.
    \]
    We can also argue by observing the surjective homomorphism 
    $\phi : \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9 \to 
    \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3$, $\phi(a,b) = (a, b\mod 3)$ 
    has kernel 
    $\ker \phi = H$ so the result follows by the first isomorphism 
    theorem.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[2 Exam 2019]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Let $\sigma = (2 \ 6 \ 1 \ 4 \ 3)$ and 
            $\tau = (5 \ 2 \ 1 \ 3)$ be permutations in $S_6$.
            Express the composition $\sigma\tau$ in disjoint 
            cycle notation.
            What is the order of $\sigma\tau$?
        \item Show that if $\sigma \in S_n$ is a cycle of odd length 
            $2k+1$, then $\sigma^2$ is also a cycle of length 
            $2k+1$.
        \item Let $G$ be a subgroup of the symmetric group 
            $S_n$ such that there exist permutations 
            $\sigma_2,\ldots,\sigma_n \in G$ satisfying 
            $\sigma_i(1) = i$ for $2 \leq i \leq n$. Show that 
            $G$ acts \textbf{transitively} on $\{1,\ldots,n\}$. In 
            other words, show that for each pair $1 \leq i,j \leq n$
            there exists a permutation $\sigma \in G$ such that 
            $\sigma(i) = j$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    $\sigma$ is $2 \to 6 \to 1 \to 4 \to 3 \to 2$ while 
    $\tau$ is $5 \to 2 \to 1 \to 3$. 
    We work out $\sigma\tau$ like this: 

    \begin{align*}
        5 \to 2 \to 6 \Rightarrow 5 \to 6 \\ 
        2 \to 1 \to 4 \Rightarrow 2 \to 4 \\ 
        1 \to 3 \to 2 \Rightarrow 1 \to 2 \\ 
        3 \to 5 \to 5 \Rightarrow 3 \to 5 \\ 
        4 \to 4 \to 3 \Rightarrow 4 \to 3 \\ 
        6 \to 6 \to 1 \Rightarrow 6 \to 1
    \end{align*}
    so $\sigma\tau = (1 \ 2 \ 4 \ 3 \ 5 \ 6)$. 
    The order of an $n$-cycle is $n$ so $|\sigma\tau| = 6$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    Suppose $\sigma$ is a cycle of length $2k+1$, i.e. 
    \[
        \sigma = (a_1 \ a_2 \ \cdots \ a_{2k+1}).
    \]
    Composing $\sigma^2$ sends $a_1 \to a_3$, $a_2 \to a_4$ and so 
    on. In particular $a_{i} \to a_{(i + 2)}$ where $i+2$ is 
    taken mod $2k+1$. Then all odd elements are taken 
    to odd elements, but the $2k+1$-th element is sent 
    to $a_2$ which is sent to $a_4$ and so on. Even elements 
    are sent to even elements until $a_{2k} \to a_1$ so 
    \[
        \sigma^2 = (a_1 \ a_3 \ \cdots \ a_{2k+1} \ a_2 \ 
        a_4 \ \cdots \ a_{2k})
    \]
    which has length $2k+1$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    Since $G$ is a group, each permutation $\sigma_k$ has an 
    inverse $\sigma_k^{-1}$ which sends $k$ to $1$.
    Also $e \in G$ so for $1 \leq i,j \leq n$ we can send 
    $i$ to $j$ by sending $i$ to $1$ and then $1$ to $j$ so 
    \[
        (\sigma_j \circ \sigma_i^{-1})(i) = 
        \sigma_j(1) = j.
    \]
    This works well for the case where neither $i,j$ are 
    $1$. If $i$ is one then let $\sigma_i := e$ and likewise 
    for $j$.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[3 Exam 2019]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that if $n$ is not prime, then $n \mathbb Z$ 
            is not a prime ideal of $\mathbb Z$.
        \item Show that the quotient 
            \[
                \frac{\mathbb Z_2[x]}{\langle x^3 + x + 1\rangle}
            \]
            is a field.
            How many elements does it contain?
        \item Find a polynomial $p(x) \in \mathbb Z_2[x]$ such that 
            $\deg(p) \leq 2$ and 
            \[
                x^5 + \langle x^3 + x + 1 \rangle
                = 
                p(x) + \langle x^3 + x + 1\rangle
            \]
            in $\frac{\mathbb Z_2[x]}{\langle x^3 + x + 1 \rangle}$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Suppose $n$ is not prime. Then $n=ab$ for some integers
    $a,b$ with $1<a<n$ and $1<b<n$. Hence
    \[
        ab=n\in n\mathbb Z.
    \]
    However, $a\notin n\mathbb Z$ and $b\notin n\mathbb Z$, since
    neither
    $a$ nor $b$ is divisible by $n$. Thus there exist 
    $a,b\in\mathbb Z$
    such that $ab\in n\mathbb Z$ but $a,b\notin n\mathbb Z$.
    Therefore $n\mathbb Z$ is not a prime ideal of $\mathbb Z$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    We will use the fact that the quotient (in this case) is a field 
    if and only if $f(x) = x^3 + x + 1$ is irreducible over 
    $\mathbb Z_2$. Notice that 
    \[
        f(0) = f(1) = 1 \neq 0
    \]
    and $f(x)$ is degree $3$ hence irreducible over 
    $\mathbb Z_2$. Thus the quotient is a field.
    There are $2^3 = 8$ elements.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    Since $x^3 + x + 1 = 0$ we have $x^3 = x + 1$. 
    Then $x^5 = x^2x^3 = x^2(x+1) = x^3 + x^2$. 
    Using the relation again we get 
    $x^5 = x^2 + x + 1$. So let $p(x) := x^2 + x + 1$. Then 
    $x^5 \langle x^3 + x + 1\rangle = p(x) + \langle x^3 + x 
    + 1\rangle$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[1 Exam 2022, (Group Theory)]
    \medskip 
    \noindent
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Let $G$ be a group of order $200$.  
            What are the sizes of Sylow $p$-subgroups of $G$? 
        \item Let $G = \mathbb Z_{12} \times \mathbb Z_8$. Find 
            the order of the element $(4,2) \in G$. 
            Determine the quotient group $G / \langle (4,2)\rangle$
            up to isomorphism.
        \item Let $p$ be a prime number. Suppose $G$ is a group 
            of order $p^n$ acting on a set $X$ such that 
            $p$ does not divide $|X|$. Show that the 
            action of $G$ must have a fixed point, i.e. 
            there exists an $x \in X$ such that $|\mathcal O| = 1$. 
    \end{enumerate}

    Note: Problem $1$ is not relevant to the current curriculum.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (2)]
    $4$ has order $3$ in $\mathbb Z_{12}$ and $2$ has order 
    $4$ in $\mathbb Z_8$ so the order of $(4,2)$ in $\mathbb Z_{12} 
    \times \mathbb Z_8$ is $\operatorname{lcm}(3,4) = 12$.

    Thus there are $12$ elements in $\langle (4,2) \rangle$ which 
    we will use to characterize the quotient group.

    $G$ has $12 \cdot 8 = 96$ elements which by Lagrange's theorem 
    gives $96/12 = 8$ elements in the quotient group.
    Now since the quotient group is a finite abelian group 
    it must be isomorphic to $\mathbb Z_8$ or $\mathbb Z_2 \times 
    \mathbb Z_2$ or $\mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_2 \times 
    \mathbb Z_2$.

    We see quickly that $(0,1)$ has order $4$ in the quotient 
    so it can't be $\mathbb Z_2^3$. Furthermore $4(a,b) \equiv (a,b)$
    when taken mod $8$ so the maximum order of any element is 
    $4$ so we conclude that the quotient is (up to isomorphism) 
    \[
        \mathbb Z_4 \times \mathbb Z_2.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (3)]
    We particion $X$ into disjoint orbits 
    $\mathcal O_1,\ldots, \mathcal O_j$. Assume, for contradiction,
    that $|\mathcal O_x| > 1$ for all $x \in X$.
    Since $|\mathcal O_x| = [G:G_x] = |G|/|G_x|$ we have that 
    $|\mathcal O_x| = p^{n_x}$ for some $n_x > 0$. Then 
    since 
    \[
        |X| = \sum_{i=1}^s |O_i|,
    \]
    we have that 
    \[
        |X| = \sum_{i=1}^s p^{n_i}.
    \]
    Then since $p$ divides every term we have 
    \[
        p \bigm| |X|
    \]
    which contradicts our assumption that $p$ does not divide 
    $|X|$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[2 Exam 2022, Ring Theory]
    \medskip 
    \noindent 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Show that $\mathbb Z_{12}$ is not an integral domain. 
        \item Let $p$ be prime and $n > 1$. Find a maximal ideal 
            $M$ of $\mathbb Z_{p^n}$ and identify the 
            quotient $\mathbb Z_{p^n} / M$. 
        \item Show that if an ideal $I$ of the polynomial ring 
            $\mathbb Q[x]$ contains both $f(x) = x^3 - x^2 + x$ 
            and $g(x) = x^2 - 1$ then $I = \mathbb Q[x]$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution sketch of (1)]
    $\mathbb Z_{12}$ contains zero-divisors 
    $3 \cdot 4 = 12 \equiv_{12} 0$ and is therefore not an 
    integral domain.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof attempt of (2)]
    We need to find an ideal $M$ so that 
    if $M \subseteq K \subseteq \mathbb Z_{p^n}$ then 
    $K = M$ or $K = \mathbb Z_{p^n}$.

    Let $M := \langle p \rangle$. Then $M$ is a maximal ideal.  

    $M$ absorbs any power of $p$ so $|\mathbb Z_{p^n}|/M| < p^n - 
    n$. Etc.   
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (3)]
    We notice that 
    \[
        \frac{x^3 - x^2 + x}{x^2 - 1} 
        = x - 1 - \frac{1}{x^2 - 1} =: r(x).
    \]
    Something, something use addition and multiplication with 
    these polynomials to get scalars, $x$ and such to be able 
    to make all polynomials over $\mathbb Q$.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}[3, Exam 2022 (Finite Fields)]
    Consider $F = \mathbb Z_3$ and the irreducible polynomial 
    $f(x) = x^3 + 2x + 2 \in F[x]$.
    Let $E$ be $F[x]/\langle f(x) \rangle$.
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item What is the size of $E$?
        \item Let $\alpha \in \overline F$ denote a zero of 
            $f(x)$ so that $E \cong F(\alpha)$. Let 
            \[
                \sigma_3 : F(\alpha) \to F(\alpha)
            \]
            denote the Frobenius automorphism defined by 
            $\sigma_3(a) = a^3$ for all $a \in F(\alpha)$.
            Write $\sigma_3(\alpha)$ and $\sigma_3(\alpha)^2$ in the 
            basis $\{1,\alpha,\alpha^2\}$ for $F(\alpha)$ over 
            $F$.
        \item Assume $g(x) = x^3 + 2x + 1 \in F[x]$ is irreducible 
            over $F$ and let $\beta \in \overline F$ be a zero of 
            $g(x)$.

            Prove that $F(\alpha)$ and $F(\beta)$ are isomorphic 
            without giving an isomorphism. However explain why 
            the map $\psi_{\alpha,\beta} : F(\alpha) \to F(\beta)$
            which fixes $F$ and sends $\alpha$ to $\beta$ cannot 
            be made into a field isomorphism.
    \end{enumerate}

\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (1)]
    $\deg f = 3$ so $|E| = 3^3 = 27$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (2)]  
    $f(\alpha) = \alpha^3 - 2 \alpha + 2 = 0$ so 
    $\alpha^3 = 2\alpha - 2 = \alpha + 1$.

    Then 
    \[
        \sigma_3(\alpha) = \alpha^3 = \alpha + 1.
    \]
    Next 
    \[
        \sigma_3^2(\alpha) = (\alpha + 1)^3 = \alpha^3 + 1 
        = \alpha + 2.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (3)]
    Since both are fields of size $27 = 3^3$ they are isomorphic.  

    The map $\alpha \to \beta$ cannot be a field isomorphism because 
    their respective minimal polynomials do not coincide.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^4 - 2 \in \mathbb Q[x].
    \]
    Find 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item the roots of $f$, 
        \item the splitting field $K$, 
        \item the degree $[K:\mathbb Q]$, 
        \item what the automorphism in $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb 
            Q)$ can do to $\sqrt[4]2$ and $i$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    \begin{align*}
        f(x) &= (x^2 - \sqrt 2)(x^2 + \sqrt 2) \\ 
             &= (x - \sqrt[4]2)(x + \sqrt[4]2)(x^2 + \sqrt 2)
    \end{align*}
    so we apply the quadratic formula to the latter term: 
    \begin{align*}
        x &= \frac{\pm \sqrt{-4\sqrt{2}}}{2} \\ 
          &= \pm \sqrt{-\sqrt{2}} \\ 
          &= \pm i \sqrt[4]{2}
    \end{align*}
    yielding 
    \[
        f(x) = (x + \sqrt[4]2)(x - \sqrt[4]2)(x + i\sqrt[4]2)
        (x - i\sqrt[4]2).
    \]
    which has roots 
    \[
        \pm \sqrt[4]2 \ \text{and} \ 
        \pm i \sqrt[4]2. 
    \]
    Thus the splitting field is 
    \[
        K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt[4]2, i).
    \]

    As we can see $f$ is irreducible over $\mathbb Q$ by 
    Eisenstein with $p = 2$ and in fact it is the minimal 
    polynomial of $\sqrt[4]2$ so 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt[4]2) : \mathbb Q] = \deg f = 4.  
    \]
    We can also see that $i \not \in \mathbb R \supset 
    \mathbb Q(\sqrt[4] 2)$ so $K$ has nontrivial degree over 
    $\mathbb Q(\sqrt[4] 2)$ and since the minimal polynomial is 
    $x^2 + 1$ we conclude that 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q(\sqrt[4]2)] = 2
    \]
    so 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q] = 8.
    \]

    Let $\alpha = \sqrt[4]2$. Then $K = \mathbb Q(\alpha, i)$ and 
    every automorphism in the Galois group is determined by 
    where it sends $\alpha$ and $i$.
    For the roots to satisfy their respective polynomials we have 
    \[
        \sigma(\alpha) \in \{\alpha, -\alpha, i\alpha, -i\alpha\}
    \]
    and 
    \[
        \sigma(i) \in \{i,-i\}.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        R = \mathbb F_3[x]/\langle x^2 + x + 2 \rangle. 
    \]

    Let 
    \[
        \alpha = x + \langle x^2 + x + 2 \rangle.
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that 
            $x^2 + x + 2$ is irreducible over 
            $\mathbb F_3$. Conclude that 
            $R$ is a field.

        \item How many elements does $R$ have? 
            Write them in the form 
            \[
                a + b\alpha, \ a,b \in\mathbb F_3. 
            \]
        \item Use the quotient relation to compute 
            \[
                \alpha^2, \alpha^3, \alpha^4. 
            \]

        \item Find the inverse of 
            \[
                1 + \alpha
            \]
            in $R$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Let $f(x) := x^2 + x + 2$. Since $\deg f \in \{2,3\}$ we 
    have that $f$ is irreducible if and only if 
    $f$ has no zeros in the ground field. 

    We quickly check that 
    \[
        f(0) = 2, \ f(1) \equiv 1, \ f(2) \equiv 2,  
    \]
    meaning $f$ has no zeros in $\mathbb F_3$ and is thus 
    irreducible. 

    Since $\mathbb F_3$ is a field we have that 
    the quotient is a field if and only if $f$ is irreducible 
    over $\mathbb F_3$. Thus we conclude that 
    $R$ is a field.
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (b)]
    We use the standard result to get 
    \[
        |R| = |\mathbb F_3|^{\deg f} = 9.
    \]

    Conclusively 
    \[
        R = \{0,1,2, \alpha, 1 + \alpha, 2 + \alpha, 
        2\alpha, 2\alpha + 1, 2\alpha + 2\}.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (c)]
    We use
    \[
        \alpha^2 + \alpha + 2 = 0
    \]
    so 
    \[
        \alpha^2 = 2\alpha + 1
    \]
    and then 
    \[
        \alpha^3 = \alpha(2\alpha + 1) 
        = 2\alpha^2 + \alpha 
        = 2(2\alpha + 1) + \alpha 
        = 2\alpha + 2   
    \]
    and lastly 
    \[
        \alpha^4 = \alpha(2\alpha + 2) 
        = 2\alpha^2 + 2\alpha 
        = 2(2\alpha + 1) + 2\alpha
        = 4\alpha + 2 + 2\alpha 
        = 2. 
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution to (c)]
    We must find $a + b\alpha$ such that 
    \[
        (1 + \alpha)(a + b\alpha) = 1.
    \]
    Expanding we get 
    \begin{align*}
        a + b\alpha + a\alpha + b\alpha^2 &= 1 \\ 
        a + a\alpha + b\alpha + 2b\alpha + b &= 1 \\ 
        a + 3b &= a \\ 
               &\; \vdots
    \end{align*}
    We get $(1 + \alpha)^{-1} = \alpha$. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        I = \langle x^2 + 1 \rangle \subseteq \mathbb Q[x].
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $I$ is maximal. 
        \item Show that 
            \[
                \mathbb Q[x]/I \cong \mathbb Q(i).
            \]
        \item Find the inverse of 
            \[
                x + 1 + I
            \]
            in the quotient ring.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    $\mathbb Q[x]$ is a PID so every ideal is generated by 
    a polynomial.
    Since $\mathbb Q$ is a field, $\langle f(x) \rangle$ is maximal 
    if and only if $f$ is irreducible over $\mathbb Q$, 
    which $x^2 + 1$ obviously is. 
\end{proof} 

\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    We will use the isomorphism theorem for rings. 

    Let $\psi : \mathbb Q[x] \to \mathbb Q(i)$ be 
    \[
        \psi(p) := p(i).
    \]
    Then very clearly $\langle x^2 + 1\rangle \subseteq 
    \ker \psi$. Now take any $p(x) \in \ker \psi$. 
    Then 
    \[
        p(x) = q(x)(x^2 + 1) + r(x),
    \]
    where 
    \[
        \deg r < 2.
    \]
    So $r(x) = ax + b$ for some $a,b \in \mathbb Q$. 
    Evaluate at $i$ to get 
    \[
        0 = p(i) = q(i)(i^2 + 1) + r(i) = ai + b,
    \]
    forcing $a = b = 0$. Thus 
    \[
        p(x) = q(x)(x^2 + 1) \in \langle x^2 + 1 \rangle.
    \]
    Hence $\ker \psi = \langle x^2 + 1 \rangle$.

    Lastly the map is surjective, i.e. $\operatorname{im} \psi = 
    \mathbb Q(i)$, 
    because take any $\gamma \in \mathbb Q(i)$. Then 
    \[
        \gamma = a + bi, \ a,b \in \mathbb Q.
    \]
    Notice then that we can find the polynomial 
    \[
        f(x) := a + bx \in \mathbb Q[x], 
    \]
    for which we have 
    \[
        \psi(f) = a + bi. 
    \]
    Thus by the first isomorphism theorem for rings, 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Q[x]}{\langle x^2 + 1\rangle} 
        \cong \mathbb Q(i).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}[Problem 3 Exam 2021]
    \ 
    \medskip

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Determine if $\mathbb Z_2[x]/\langle x^4 + 1 \rangle$ 
            is an integral domain.
        \item Show that $f(x) = x^4 + x^3 + 1$ is 
            irreducible in $\mathbb Z_2[x]$. Explain why 
            $f(x)$ admits a zero $\theta$ in the field 
            $\mathbb Z_{16}$, seen as an extension of 
            $\mathbb Z_2$, with degree 
            $[\mathbb Z_{16} : \mathbb Z_2] = 4$. Prove that 
            $f(x)$ is the primitive polynomial in $\mathbb Z_2[x]$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    We notice that, in $\mathbb Z_2[x]$, 
    \[
        x^4 + 1 = x^4 - 1 = (x^2 + 1)(x^2 - 1)
    \]
    and so is the product of two lower-degree (yet non-zero) 
    polynomials in $\mathbb Z_2[x]$, and is thus not 
    irreducible. From this we can very quickly 
    see that $\langle x^4 + 1 \rangle$ is not a 
    prime ideal, meaning $\mathbb Z_2[x]/\langle x^4 + 1 \rangle$
    is not an integral domain.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    We see that $f$ has no zeros in $\mathbb Z_2$ and that 
    any factorization into two 
    degree-$2$ polynomials 
    \[
        f(x) = (x^2 + ax + b)(x^2 + cx + d)
    \]
    requires $b=d=1$, but then $1 = a+c = 0$.
    Therefore $f$ is irreducible over the ground field.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 + x + 1 \in \mathbb F_2[x],
    \]
    and let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle.
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        \theta = x + \langle f \rangle \in K.
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f$ is irreducible over 
            $\mathbb F_2$. Conclude $K$ is a field.
        \item Use 
            \[
                f(\theta) = 0
            \]
            to express $\theta^3$
            in terms of $1,\theta, \theta^2$.
        \item Determine whether $\theta$ is a generator 
            of $K^\times$. 
        \item Write the factorization of $f$ into linear 
            factors over $K$ using Frobenius conjugates.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Since $f$ is degree-$3$ we have irreducible if and only 
    if no zeros. We notice that 
    \[
        f(0) = f(1) = 1.
    \]
    Thus $f$ is irreducible over $\mathbb F_2$.

    Since $\mathbb F_2$ is a field we have that the quotient 
    \[
        \mathbb F_2/\langle f \rangle
    \]
    is a field if and only if $f$ is irreducible over 
    $\mathbb F_2$. Thus $K$ is a field with 
    \[
        |K| = 2^3 = 8.
    \]  
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    We have 
    \[
        \theta^3 + \theta + 1 = 0
    \]
    meaning 
    \[
        \theta^3 = -\theta - 1 = \theta + 1.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    $\langle f \rangle$ is maximal so 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb F_2}{\langle f \rangle}
    \]
    can be viewed as a field-extension of $\mathbb F_2$ of 
    degree $\deg f = 3$, with the 
    basis $\{1, \theta, \theta^2\}$. We know 
    \[
        |K| = 8
    \]
    so 
    \[
        K \cong \mathbb F_8.
    \]
    Now the question is whether 
    \[
        \langle \theta \rangle 
        \cong K^\times \cong \mathbb F_7.
    \]
    We compute the lowest $j$ such that
    \[
        \theta^j = 1.
    \]
    We see that 

    \begin{align*}
        \theta^7 
        &= \theta^2\theta^2\theta^3 \\ 
        &= \theta^4(\theta + 1) \\ 
        &= \theta^5 + \theta^4 \\ 
        &= \theta^2(\theta + 1) + \theta^4 \\ 
        &= \theta^3 + \theta + 2\theta^2 \\ 
        &= \theta^3 + \theta \\ 
        &= -1 \\ 
        &= 1. 
    \end{align*}
    Doing further computations for $\theta^j$ for $j < 7$ we see 
    that this is indeed the order of $\theta$. Thus 
    \[
        |\langle \theta \rangle| = 7 = |\mathbb F_7|
    \]
    and we conclude that 
    $\theta$ does indeed generate the multiplicative group.

\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^4 + x + 1 \in \mathbb F_2[x],
    \]
    and define 
    \[
        K = \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle.
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        \alpha = x + \langle f(x) \rangle \in K.
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f(x)$ is irreducible over 
            $\mathbb F_2$.
        \item Conclude that $K$ is a field. How many elements does 
            $K$ have? 
            Give a basis for $K$ as a vector space over 
            $\mathbb F_2$.
        \item Use $f(\alpha) = 0$ to compute 
            \[
                \alpha^4, \ \alpha^5, 
                \ \alpha^6, \ \alpha^7.
            \]
            Write each in the basis from $b)$.
        \item Determine whether $\alpha$ generates 
            $K^\times$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Suppose $f$ is reducible. Then it factors into two 
    degree-$2$ polynomials 
    \[
        f(x) = x^4 + x + 1 = (x^2 + ax + b)(x^2 + cx + d).
    \]
    Then we require $bd = 1$ so $b = d = 1$, and 
    \[
        adx + bcx = (ad + bc)x = x, 
    \]
    so 
    \[
        ad + bc = 1
    \]
    and since $b = d = 1$ we then have 
    \[
        a + c = 1.
    \]
    Then either $a=1$ or $c = 1$ while the other 
    is $0$. Suppose wlog $a = 1$ and $c = 0$. Then 
    \[
        x^4 + x + 1 = (x^2 + x + 1)(x^2 + 1) 
        = x^4 + x^2 + x^3 + x + x^2 + 1 
        = x^4 + x^3 + x + 1
    \]
    which is false. Our assumption was incorrect and 
    $f$ is irreducible. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    As $f$ is irreducible we conclude 
    $\langle f \rangle$ is a maximal ideal of 
    $\mathbb F_2[x]$. Since $\mathbb F_2$ is a field 
    (and therefore a commutative ring), we have that 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f \rangle} 
    \]
    is a field. 
    
    Since $\mathbb F_2$ is a finite field, and any 
    non-trivial quotient of the polynomial ring over a finite 
    field by an irreducible polynomial is a finite field 
    extension, we conclude 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f \rangle} 
    \]
    must be a (finite) field extension.

    The standard result is that 
    \[
        \left| \frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f \rangle} \right| 
        = |\mathbb F_2|^{\deg f} 
        = 16.
    \]
    The standard basis is then 
    \[
        1, \alpha, \alpha^2, \alpha^3
    \]
    since 
    \[
        \left[\frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f \rangle} : \mathbb F_2
            \right] 
        = 4.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    We have that 
    \[
        \alpha^4 + \alpha + 1 = 0, 
    \]
    so 
    \[
        \alpha^4 = \alpha + 1.
    \]
    Then 
    \[
        \alpha^5 = \alpha(\alpha + 1) = \alpha^2 + \alpha,
    \]
    and 
    \[
        \alpha^6 = \alpha(\alpha^2 + \alpha) = \alpha^3 + \alpha^2. 
    \]
    Lastly 
    \[
        \alpha^7 = \alpha^4 + \alpha^3 
        = \alpha^3 + \alpha + 1.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    We have that 
    \[
        K := \frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f \rangle} 
        \cong \mathbb F_{16},  
    \]
    since the finite fields are classified up to isomorphism. 
    Then the question is whether 
    \[
        \langle \alpha \rangle 
        = K^\times \cong \mathbb F_{16}^\times \cong 
        C_{15} \leq \mathbb F_{16}.
    \]
    Then if we find that $|\langle \alpha \rangle| = 15$ we 
    will be finished. 
    First we can check whether 
    $\alpha^{15} = 1$ as we would expect. 

    \begin{align*}
        \alpha^{15} 
        &= \alpha^8(\alpha^3 + \alpha + 1) \\ 
        &= \alpha^{11} + \alpha^9 + \alpha^8 \\ 
        &= (\alpha^5 \alpha^6) + \alpha^3\alpha^3 + (\alpha + 1)^2 \\ 
        &= (\alpha^2 + \alpha)(\alpha^3 + \alpha^2) + 
        \alpha^3 \alpha^3 + \alpha^2 + 1 \\ 
        &= \alpha^5 + \alpha^4 + \cdots 
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $G = C_4 = \langle r \rangle$ act on the set 
    \[
        X = \{0,1\}^4
    \]
    of binary strings of length $4$ by cyclic rotation.
    Thus 
    \[
        r(a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4) = (a_4,a_1,a_2,a_3).
    \]
    In other words, two binary strings are considered 
    equivalent if one can be obtained from the other by 
    rotating the positions.

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Write down the elements of $G$ and describe how 
            each acts on $X$. 
        \item For each $g \in G$, compute the number of fixed 
            points 
            \[
                |X^g| = \{x \in X : gx = x\}.
            \]
        \item Use Burnside's formula to determine the number 
            of orbits. 
        \item Interpret the answer: how many 
            binary of necklaces of length $4$ are there, up to 
            rotation?
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution and proof to all]
    $G = \{e, r, r^2, r^3\}$.
    Suppose $(a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4) \in X$. Then 
    \[
        r(a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4) = (a_4,a_1,a_2,a_3)
    \]
    and 
    \[
        r^2(a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4) = r(a_4,a_1,a_2,a_3) 
        = (a_3,a_4,a_1,a_2)
    \]
    and 
    \[
        r^3(a_1,a_2,a_3,a_4) = (a_2,a_3,a_4,a_1)
    \]
    with $e$ acting on $X$ by doing nothing.

    $X^e = X$ as always. 

    $X^r = \{(a,a,a,a) : a \in \{0,1\}\} = \{(0,0,0,0),(1,1,1,1)\}$. 
    This is quickly seen since we require $a_1 = a_4$ and 
    $a_2 = a_1$, but then $a_2 = a_4$ and since $a_3 = a_2$ they 
    must all be equal.
    By a similar argument we get the same result for 
    $X^{r^3}$. 

    However, $X^{r^2}$ is different since it just swaps 
    entries $1$ and $2$ with $3$ and $4$, respectively. 

    Thus 
    \[
        X^{r^2} = \{(a,b,a,b) : a,b \in \{0,1\}\}
    \]
    which means $|X^{r^2}| = 2^2 = 4$.

    The number of orbits, by Burnside's lemma/formula is given by 
    \[
        |X/G| = \frac{1}{|G|}\displaystyle\sum_{g \in G}
        |X^g|.
    \]
    Meaning we get 
    \[
        |X/G| = \frac{1}{4} 
        (\underbrace{|X^e|}_{|X|} + \underbrace{|X^r|}_{2} + 
        \underbrace{|X^{r^2}|}_{4} + \underbrace{|X^{r^3}|}_{2})
        = \frac{1}{4}(16 + 2 + 4 + 2) = 24/4 = 6.
    \]

    Thus there are $6$ necklaces unique, up to rotation, when 
    you have two types of pearls, which is quick to verify mentally. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $G = D_4$ be the symmetries of the square, 
    acting on the set 
    \[
        X = \{\text{colorings of the four vertices of a 
        square using red/blue}\}.
    \]
    We will say that two colorings are the same if 
    one can be obtained from the other by rotating 
    or reflecting the square. 

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item List the elements of $D_4$, and describe their 
            action on the vertices. 
        \item Compute the number of fixed colorings 
            $|X^g|$ for each type of symmetry $g \in D_4$.
        \item Use Burnside's formula to compute 
            the number of orbits.
        \item Pick the coloring 
            \[
                x = (R,R,B,B)
            \]
            around the square. Find the orbit and stabilizer.
            Verify orbit-stabilizer: 
            \[
                |Gx| = \frac{|G|}{|G_x|}. 
            \]
        \item Explain in words how the orbit-stabilizer theorem 
            and Burnside's formula are related, but different.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof and solution of all]
    $D_4$ is the set of rigid symmetries of the square. 
    We can rotate the square $r$ and reflect it $s$. 
    Rotating $4$ times is the same as doing something so 
    $|r| = 4$. Then also $s^2 = e$ and $srs = r^{-1}$.
    Then 
    \[
        D_4 = \{r,s \bigm| r^4 = e, \ s^2 = e, \ srs = r^{-1}\}
        = 
        \{e, r,r^2,r^3,s,rs, r^2s, r^3s\}.
    \]
    Rotating a square $(1,2,3,4)$ gives 
    \[
        r(1,2,3,4) = (4,1,2,3)
    \]
    and reflecting gives 
    \[
        s(1,2,3,4) = (2,1,4,3). 
    \]
    So we can think of these actions as the bijections 
    acting on the indices of the vertices
    $r : \mathbb Z_4 \to \mathbb Z_4$, 
    $r(i) := i + 1 \mod 4$, 
    \[
        s(i) = 
        \begin{cases}
            i + 1 \mod 4, & \text{if odd}\ i \\ 
            i - 1 \mod 4, & \text{if even}\ i
        \end{cases}.
    \]
    Then $r^n(i) = i + n \mod 4$ and 
    \[
        s^n(i)
        = 
        \begin{cases}
            s(i), & \text{if} \ n \ \text{odd} \\ 
            i , & \text{if} \ n \ \text{even}
        \end{cases}.
    \]

    The number of colorings for $e$ is $|X^e| = |X|$. 

    For $g = r$ we, by the same argument as in the necklace problem,
    \[
        |X^r| = 2. 
    \]

    For $g = r^3 = r^{-1}$ we get the same result. 

    For $r^2$ we get 
    \[
        X^{r^2} = \{(a,b,a,b) : a,b \in \{R,B\}\},
    \]
    so 
    \[
        |X^{r^2}| = 4.
    \]

    For $g = s$ we once again get 
    \[
        |X^s| = 4.
    \]

    For $g = rs$ we get 
    \[
        r \circ s(1,2,3,4) = r(2,1,4,3) = 
        (3,2,1,4)
    \]
    which is fixed only if $1 = 3$. Meaning we have $2$ choices 
    for indices $1$ and $3$ and $2$ for index $2$ and likewise for 
    index $4$. Thus $|X^{rs}| = 2^3 = 8$.

    For $g = r^2s$ we get 
    \[
        r^2 \circ s (1,2,3,4) = r(3,2,1,4) = (4,3,2,1). 
    \]
    Here we have $1 \mapsto 4$ and vice versa, likewise for 
    $2$ and $3$. Thus we get $|X^{r^2s}| = 4$. 

    Lastly for 
    $g = r^3s$ we have 
    \[
        r^3 s(1,2,3,4) = r(4,3,2,1) = (1,4,3,2)
    \]
    for which $1$ and $3$ are fixed and $2$ and $4$ are swapped. 
    Like with $g = rs$ we get 
    $|X^{r^3s}| = 8$.

    Then by Burnside's lemma, the number of orbits is 
    \[
        \#(G/X) = 
        \frac{1}{8} (16 + 2 + 2 + 4 + 4 + 8 + 4 + 8) 
        = \frac{48}{8} = 6.
    \]

    Let $x = (R,R,B,B)$. The orbit is 
    \[
        \{x, (B,R,R,B), (B,B,R,R), (R,B,B,R)\}
    \]
    with $|\operatorname{Orb}_G(x)| = 4$.
    Then the stabilizer is 
    \[
        \{e,s\}.
    \]
    Then we have 
    \[
        |G| = 8 = 2 \cdot 4 = |\operatorname{Orb}_G(x)| \cdot 
        |\operatorname{Stab}_G(x)|.
    \]

    Burnside's formula relates the number of orbits to 
    the number of elements fixed by elements in $G$ while 
    the orbit-stabilizer theorem relates 
    elements of $G$ which fix $x \in X$, what 
    elements can be reached by $x$ through the action of the group 
    and states that the product of those will be the size of the 
    group (when finite). The easiest example is a group acting on 
    itself and seeing that $e$ can reach every element in the 
    group ($|G|$ elements), but is only fixed by itself, 
    $|\{e\}| = 1$. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 + x + 1 \in \mathbb F_2[x],
    \]
    and let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb F[x]/\langle f(x) \rangle.
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        \alpha = x + \langle f(x) \rangle.
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f$ is irreducible over 
            $\mathbb F_2$. Conclude that $K$ is a field.
        \item Use 
            \[
                f(\alpha) = 0
            \]
            to compute 
            \[
                \alpha^3, \alpha^4, 
                \alpha^5, \alpha^6, \alpha^7.
            \]
            in the basis $\{1,\alpha,\alpha^2\}$.
        \item The Frobenius automorphism is
            \[
                \sigma_2 : K \to K, \ \sigma_2(a) = a^2.
            \]
            Compute 
            \[
                \sigma_2(\alpha), \ \sigma_2^2(\alpha), \ 
                \sigma_2^3(\alpha).
            \]
        \item Use Frobenius conjugates to factor 
            $f(x)$ into linear factors over $K$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Since $\deg f \in \{2,3\}$ we can use the fact that $f$ is 
    irreducible over the ground field if and only if 
    it has no zeros in said field. 
    Since 
    \[
        f(0) = 1 = f(1)
    \]
    we have that $f$ is irreducible over $\mathbb F_2$.

    As $\mathbb F_2[x]$ is a field and $\langle f \rangle$ is 
    a maximal ideal by $f$ being reducible we have that 
    \[
        \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle
    \]
    is a field. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    \begin{align*}
        f(\alpha) 
        &= 0 \\ 
        \alpha^3 + \alpha + 1 
        &= 0 \\ 
        \alpha^3 
        &= \alpha + 1. 
    \end{align*}
    Then 
    \begin{align*}
        \alpha^4 &= \alpha^2 + \alpha \\ 
        \alpha^5 &= \alpha^3 + \alpha^2 \\ 
                 &= \alpha^2 + \alpha + 1 \\ 
        \alpha^6 &= \alpha(\alpha^2 + \alpha + 1) \\ 
                 &= \alpha + 1 + \alpha^2 + \alpha \\ 
                 &= \alpha^2 + 1  \\ 
        \alpha^7 &= \alpha + 1 + \alpha \\ 
                 &= 1.
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma_2(\alpha) &= \alpha^2 \\ 
        \sigma_2^2(\alpha) &= \sigma_2(\alpha^2) \\ 
                           &= \alpha^4 \\ 
                           &= \alpha^2 + \alpha \\ 
        \sigma_2^3(\alpha) &= \sigma(\alpha^2 + \alpha) \\ 
                           &= (\alpha^2 + \alpha)(\alpha^2 + \alpha)
                           \\ 
                           &= \alpha^4 + 2\alpha^3 + \alpha^2 \\ 
                           &= \alpha^2 + \alpha + \alpha^2 \\ 
                           &= \alpha.
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (d)]
    Since $f$ has coefficients in $\mathbb F_2$, Frobenius 
    sends roots to roots. Therefore the roots of $K$ are 
    \[
        \alpha, \ \alpha^2, \ \alpha^4.
    \]
    Writing this in the basis we get roots 
    \[
        \alpha, \ \alpha^2, \ \alpha^2 + \alpha.
    \]
    Thus
