    \[
        f(x) = (x-\alpha)(x-\alpha^2)(x-\alpha^4) 
        = (x+\alpha)(x+\alpha^2)(x+\alpha^4).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 - 5 \in \mathbb Q[x].
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        \alpha = \sqrt[3]5
    \]
    be the real cube root of $5$, and let 
    \[
        \zeta = \zeta_3 = e^{2i\pi/3}.
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f(x)$ is irreducible over the 
            rationals. 
        \item Find all roots of $f(x)$ in $\mathbb C$, and 
            describe the splitting field $K$ of $f$ over 
            $\mathbb Q$.
        \item Compute 
            \[
                [K:\mathbb Q].
            \]
        \item Show that 
            \[
                \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \cong S_3.
            \]
        \item Describe the two automorphisms of $K$ that generate 
            the Galois group.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Since $f$ is over $\mathbb Q$ with integer coefficients we can 
    use Eistenstein's criterion. Let $p = 5$. Then 
    $p$ divides $5$, but not the leading coefficient $1$ and 
    $p^2 = 25$ does not divide $5$. Thus $f$ is irreducible over 
    $\mathbb Q$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b) and (c)]
    We have the obvious 
    \[
        f(\sqrt[3]5) = 0.
    \]
    Then we notice that 
    \[
        f(\zeta_3\sqrt[3]5) = 
        e^{2\pi}\cdot 5 - 5 
        = 0.
    \]
    Thus the roots in $\mathbb C$ are 
    \[
        \sqrt[3]5 \ \text{and} \ \zeta_3\sqrt[3]5.
    \]
    Thus the splitting field is 
    \[
        K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt[3]5, \zeta_3).
    \]
    Noticing that $f$ is actually the minimal polynomial of 
    $\sqrt[3]5$ we have 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt[3]5) : \mathbb Q] = 3. 
    \]
    Furthermore, generally for an $n$-th root of unity the minimal 
    polynomial is 
    \[
        \prod_{d|n} (x^{n/d})^\mu(d)
    \]
    where $\mu$ is the Möbius function. 
    So the minimal polynomial of $\zeta_3$ over $\mathbb Q(\sqrt[3]
    5)$ is 
    \[
        x^2 + x + 1
    \]  
    meaning, by the tower law,  
    \[
        [K:\mathbb Q] 
        = 2 \cdot 3 = 6.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (d)]
    For the splitting field for a degree $n$ polynomial we have 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/F) \leq S_n
    \]
    so 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \leq S_3. 
    \]
    Since $K/\mathbb Q$ is Galois we have 
    \[
        |\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)|
        = [K:\mathbb Q] = 6 = |S_3|. 
    \]
    The only subgroup of $S_3$ with $|S_3|$ elements is $S_3$ itself.
    Hence 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)
        \cong S_3.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (e)]
    Define $\sigma(\alpha) = \zeta\alpha$ and 
    $\sigma(\zeta) = \zeta$. Then 
    \[
        \sigma(\alpha) = \zeta\alpha
    \]
    and
    \[
        \sigma(\zeta\alpha) = \zeta^2\alpha
    \]
    and 
    \[
        \sigma(\zeta^2\alpha) = \alpha.
    \]
    So $\sigma$ permutes the three roots, and has 
    order $3$.

    Define $\tau(\alpha) = \alpha$, and 
    $\tau(\sigma) = \sigma^2$.
    Then 
    \[
        \tau(\alpha) = \alpha, 
    \]
    and 
    \[
        \tau(\alpha\sigma) = \alpha\sigma^2
    \]
    and 
    \[
        \tau(\alpha\sigma^2) = \alpha\sigma.
    \]
    Thus $\tau$ is complex conjugation and since $\alpha$ is fixed 
    it has order $2$.

    If we label $\alpha = 1, \alpha\zeta = 2, \alpha\zeta^2 = 3$ 
    we get 
    \[
        \sigma = (1 \ 2 \ 3), \ \tau = (2 \ 3)
    \]
    so a $3$-cycle and a transposition, which generate 
    $S_3$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 + x + 1 \in \mathbb F_2[x],
    \]
    and let 
    \[
        K = \frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f(x) \rangle}.
    \]
    Write 
    \[
        \alpha = x + \langle f(x) \rangle \in K.
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f(x)$ is irreducible over 
            $\mathbb F_2$.
        \item Conclude that $K$ is a field. How many elements 
            does $K$ have? Give a basis for $K$ as a vector space 
            over $\mathbb F_2$.
        \item Use $f(\alpha) = 0$ to compute 
            \[
                \alpha^3, \ \alpha^4, \ \alpha^5, \ \alpha^6, 
                \ \alpha^7.
            \]
            Write each in the basis. 
        \item Show that $\alpha$ generates $K^\times$. 
        \item Let $\sigma : K \to K$, $\sigma(a) = a^2$. 
            Show that $\sigma$ is an automorphism of $K$ and 
            compute 
            \[
                \sigma(\alpha), \ \sigma^2(\alpha), 
                \ \sigma^3(\alpha).
            \]
        \item Determine the Galois group 
            \[
                \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2).
            \]
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Since $\deg f \in \{2,3\}$ we have that 
    $f$ is irreducible if and only if it 
    has no zeros in the ground-field 
    $\mathbb F_2$. 
    We quickly see that 
    \[
        f(0) = 1 = f(1)
    \]
    so $f$ has no zeros 
    in $\mathbb F_2$ and is 
    thus irreducible. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    Since $\mathbb F_2[x]$ is a PID 
    we have that each ideal is generated 
    by some $f$, and every non-zero prime ideal 
    is a maximal ideal. Thus $\langle f \rangle$ is 
    a maximal ideal if and only if $f$ is 
    irreducible, which we have shown. 
    Since $\langle f \rangle$ is maximal we 
    then have that $K$ is a field. 

    We have $|K| = |\mathbb F_2|^{\deg f} = 8$.
    This is because since, for $g(x) \in \mathbb F_2[x]$, 
    we have $g(x) = f(x)q(x) + r(x)$ where 
    $\deg r < \deg f$. Therefore every 
    element in the quotient can be written 
    as a linear combination of 
    \[
        1, x, x^2 
    \]
    with scalars in $\mathbb F_2$. 
    So we have $2$ choices for $1$, 
    $2$ for $x$ and $2$ for $x^2$. 
    Hence $|K| = 2^3 = 8$.

    If $\alpha = x + \langle f(x) \rangle$, we have 
    the standard basis 
    $\{1, \alpha, \alpha^2\}$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    We have 
    \[
        \alpha^3 + \alpha + 1 = 0
    \]
    so rearranging, and recalling that 
    addition and subtraction are interchangable 
    in $\mathbb F_2$ we get 
    \[
        \alpha^3 = \alpha + 1. 
    \]
    Then 
    \[
        \alpha^4 = \alpha^2 + \alpha. 
    \]
    Next 
    \[
        \alpha^5 = \alpha^3 + \alpha^2 = \alpha^2 + \alpha + 1. 
    \]
    Next 
    \[
        \alpha^6 = \alpha^3 + \alpha^2 + \alpha 
        = \alpha^2 + 1. 
    \]
    Last 
    \[
        \alpha^7 = \alpha^3 + \alpha 
        = 1. 
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    We have that $|\alpha| = 7$ by (c), so 
    $|\langle \alpha \rangle| = 7$.
    We have that $|K| = 8$ and since 
    the finite fields are characterized up 
    to isomorphism we have 
    \[
        K \cong \mathbb F_8
    \]
    which means that $K^\times \cong C_7 
    \leq \mathbb F_8$ (as groups).
    Since $\alpha$ generates 
    a subgroup of $\mathbb F_8$ which has 
    $7$ elements and the only subgroup of 
    $\mathbb F_8$ with $7$ elements is 
    $C_7$ we conclude that 
    \[
        \langle \alpha \rangle 
        \cong C_7 
        \cong K.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[e]
    We confirm the homomorphism properties first. 
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma(a+b) 
        &= (a+b)^2 \\ 
        &= a^2 + 2ab + b^2 \\ 
        &= a^2 + b^2 \\ 
        &= \sigma(a) + \sigma(b)
    \end{align*}
    since $2 \equiv 0$. 
    Then 
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma(ab) 
        &= (ab)^2 \\ 
        &= a^2b^2 \\ 
        &= \sigma(a)\sigma(b).
    \end{align*}

    Let $\phi : \mathbb F_8 \to \mathbb F_8$ defined 
    by $\phi(a) = a^2$. Then suppose 
    $\phi(a) = 0$. Then $a^2 = 0$ so 
    $a = 0$. This means the map is injective since 
    $\ker \phi = \{0\}$. Furthermore we se see that 
    \[
        0 \mapsto 0, \ 1 \mapsto 1, \ 2 \mapsto 4, \ldots
    \] 
    and we can confirm for ourselves in this way that 
    the map is indeed bijective. 
    Now let $\psi$ be the isomorphism from 
    $K$ to $\mathbb F_8$. Such a map exists since 
    we have established that $K \cong \mathbb F_8$. 
    Then $\sigma : K \to K = \psi \circ \phi \circ \psi^{-1}$, i.e. 
    a composition of isomorphisms and therefore an isormorphism 
    on $K$. Hence $\sigma$ is an automorphism of 
    $K$.

    Then 
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma(\alpha) 
        &= \alpha^2 \\ 
        \sigma^2(\alpha ) 
        &= \alpha^4 = \alpha^2 + \alpha \\ 
        \sigma^3(\alpha) &= 
        \alpha^8 = \alpha(\alpha^7) = \alpha. 
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (f)]
    We use the standard result that 
    for $\operatorname{Gal}
    (\mathbb F_{q^n}/\mathbb F_q)$, we have 
    that the galois group is generated by the 
    $s$-th power of the 
    Frobenius automorphism where $s$ is the 
    characteristic of the field. 
    Thus 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2) 
        = \langle \sigma \rangle.
    \]
    As we saw we have 
    $|\sigma^2| = 3$ which is actually 
    inline with $[K : \mathbb F_2]$ even 
    though the extension is not automatically 
    Galois as it would be if we we're working over 
    $\mathbb Q$ for instance.

    In particular we have 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2) 
        = \mathbb Z/3 \mathbb Z.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        \phi : \mathbb Z[x] \to \mathbb Z_6
    \]
    be the ring homomorphism defined by 
    \[
        \phi(f(x)) = f(2) \mod 6. 
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $\phi$ is a ring homomorphism. 
        \item Find $\ker \phi$. 
            Try to describe it as an ideal generated by 
            simple elements of $\mathbb Z[x]$. 
        \item Identify 
            \[
                \mathbb Z[x] / \ker \phi. 
            \]
        \item Is $\ker \phi$ prime? 
        \item Is $\ker \phi$ maximal? 
        \item Replace $\mathbb Z_6$ with $\mathbb Z_5$. Define 
            \[
                \psi : \mathbb Z[x] \to \mathbb Z_5
            \]
            by 
            \[
                \psi(f(x)) = f(2) \mod 5. 
            \]
            Find the kernel, and decide whether it is 
            prime or maximal.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    $\ker \phi = \{f(x) \in \mathbb Z[x] : \phi(f(x)) = 0\}$. This set includes 
    $f(x) = 6n \cdot g(x), \ n \in \mathbb Z, \ g(x) \in \mathbb Z[x]$,
    $(x-2) \cdot g(x), \ g(x) \in \mathbb Z[x]$. 
    It is therefore not hard to see that 
    \[
        \ker \phi = \langle x-2, 6 \rangle, 
    \]
    since $x-2, 6 \in \ker \phi$ and for some 
    $f(x) \in \ker \phi$ we have that 
    $f(x) = 0$ for $x = 2$ or $f(x) \equiv 0$ 
    modulo $6$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    By the first isomorphism theorem we have 
    \[
        \mathbb Z[x] / \ker \phi \cong \operatorname{im} \phi
    \]
    and since $\phi$ is trivially surjective 
    (for $n \in \mathbb Z_6$ consider the plynomial 
    $f(x) = n$), we have that 
    \[
        \mathbb Z[x] / \ker \phi \cong \mathbb Z_6.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (d) and (e)]
    Since $\mathbb Z[x]$ is a commutative ring 
    we have that $\mathbb Z[x]/\ker \phi$ is 
    an integral domain iff $\ker \phi$ is 
    prime and said quotient ring is 
    a field iff $\ker \phi$ is maximal.
    As we have identified the quotient ring 
    to be isomorphic to $\mathbb Z_6$ we can 
    answer no to both of these questions since 
    $2 \cdot 3 = 6 \equiv 0$ which disqualifies 
    the quotient ring from being an integral domain 
    which disqualifies it from being a field. 
    
    Thus $\ker \phi$ is neither maximal nor prime. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (f)]
    By the first isomorphism theorem and the same 
    arguments as earlier we have 
    \[
        \mathbb Z[x]/\ker \psi \cong \mathbb Z_5
    \]
    and $\mathbb Z_5$ is indeed a field 
    (since $\mathbb Z_q$ is a field when $q$ 
    is prime) and thus $\ker \psi$ is maximal 
    which implies that it is also prime.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 - 2 \in \mathbb Q[x]. 
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        \alpha = \sqrt[3]2
    \]
    be the real cube root of $2$, 
    and let 
    \[
        \omega = e^{2\pi/3}
    \]
    be a primitive third root of unity. 
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f(x)$ is irreducible over 
            $\mathbb Q$.
        \item Find all roots of $f$ in 
            $\mathbb C$, and show that the splitting 
            field is 
            \[
                K = \mathbb Q(\alpha, \omega). 
            \]
        \item Compute 
            \[
                [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q].
            \]
        \item Conclude that 
            \[
                [K : \mathbb Q] = 6.
            \]
        \item Show that every $\mathbb Q$-automorphism 
            of $K$ is determined by the images 
            of $\alpha$ and $\omega$.
        \item Construct two automorphisms $\sigma,\tau 
            \in \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)$ by 
            \[
                \sigma(\alpha) = \omega\alpha, \ \sigma(\omega) = \omega, 
            \]
            and 
            \[
                \tau(\alpha) = \alpha, \ \tau(\omega) = \omega^2.
            \]
            Show that these are valid automorphisms. 
        \item Compute 
            \[
                \sigma^3, \ \tau^2, \tau\sigma\tau^{-1}.
            \]
            Conclude that 
            \[
                \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)\cong S_3.
            \]
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    $f$ is a polynomial with integer coefficients so 
    we can use Eisenstein's criterion. 
    We see that for $p = 2$ we have 
    \[
        p \mid a_0 = -2 
    \]
    and the other terms 
    which are $0$
    while 
    \[
        p \nmid a_3 = 1, 
    \]
    and 
    \[
        4 = p^2 \nmid a_0 = 2.
    \]
    Thus, by Eisenstein's criterion, $f$ is irreducible 
    over $\mathbb Q$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    Over $\mathbb C$ we have 
    \[
        f(\alpha) = f(\sqrt[3]2) = (\sqrt[3]2)^3 - 2 = 0, 
    \]
    and 
    \[
        f(\omega\alpha) = f(\omega\sqrt[3]2) = 0.  
    \]
    These are the obvious ones. Next up we see that 
    \[
        f(\omega^2\alpha) = 0
    \]
    as well.
    Then, we have 
    \[
        K \subseteq \mathbb Q(\alpha, \omega),
    \]
    since $\alpha,\omega\alpha,\omega^2\alpha \in 
    \mathbb Q(\alpha,\omega)$.
    
    For the other direction we can see that 
    $\omega,\alpha$ generate $K$ when 
    adjoining to $\mathbb Q$ so 
    $E \subseteq K$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    We have that 
    $f$ is the minimal polynomial 
    of $\sqrt[3]2$ over $\mathbb Q$ so 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q] = 
        \deg f = 3. 
    \]
    Now since $\omega \in \mathbb C$ 
    we have $\omega \not\in \mathbb Q(\alpha)$ 
    because $\mathbb Q(\alpha)$. 
    We can see that this is the case because 
    \[
        \mathbb Q(\alpha) 
        = \{a + b\sqrt[3]2 \bigm| a,b \in \mathbb Q\}, 
    \]
    and $a,b,\sqrt[3]2 \in \mathbb R$. 
    Since $\mathbb C \supset 
    \mathbb R\supset \mathbb Q(\alpha)$ we conclude 
    that the claim is true.

    The minimal polynomial of an 
    $n$-th root of unity is 
    \[
        \prod_{d| n}(x^d - 1)^{\mu(n/d)}
    \]
    which, for prime $n$ works out nicely to 
    \[
        \sum_{0 \leq k \leq n-1} x^n. 
    \]
    In this case the primitive polynomial is 
    \[
        x^2 + x + 1
    \]
    and thus 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha,\omega) : \mathbb Q(\alpha)] = 2. 
    \]
    Therefore, by the tower law, we have 
    \[
        [\mathbb K : \mathbb Q] = 2 \cdot 3 = 6.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    A $\mathbb Q$-automorphism of $K$ 
    is $\psi : K \to K$ such that 
    \[
        \psi[\mathbb Q] = \mathbb Q. 
    \]
    Thus, for every nonidentity $\mathbb Q$-automorphism 
    on $K$ we have 
    \[
        \psi(\alpha) = \beta
    \]
    where $\alpha,\beta$ are roots of $f$. 

    We have every root is sent to some root so 
    the images of $\omega$ must be fixed 
    or sent to $\omega^2$. If $\omega \mapsto 
    \alpha$, for instance we would have 
    $\psi(\omega\alpha) = \psi(\alpha^2) \neq 0$. 
    $\alpha$ can be sent to $\alpha\omega$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (f)]
    We will show $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \cong 
    S_3$ first. 
    First since $K$ is algebraic over $\mathbb Q$ and 
    the splitting field of 
    a $\mathbb Q[x]$ polynomial and 
    $\mathbb Q$ is characteristic $0$ we get 
    that $K$ is a normal- (by the former) and 
    separable (by the latter) extension, since 
    every algebraic axtension of a characteristic $0$  
    field is separable.
    
    Thus $K/\mathbb Q$ is Galois and therefore 
    \[
        [K:\mathbb Q] = |\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)|. 
    \]
    Since $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \leq S_3$ (from $K$ being 
    splitting field over $\deg f = 3$ polynomial) we have 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \cong S_3.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3).
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Prove carefully that 
            \[
                [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2):\mathbb Q] = 2.
            \]
            Then prove that 
            \[
                \sqrt 3 \not\in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2). 
            \]
            Conclude that 
            \[
                [K : \mathbb Q] = 4. 
            \]
        \item Show that 
            \[
                K
            \] 
            is the splitting field over $\mathbb Q$ of 
            \[
                f(x) = (x^2 - 2)(x^2 - 3).
            \]
            Conclude that 
            \[
                K/\mathbb Q
            \]
            is Galois.
        \item Show that every $\mathbb Q$-automorphism 
            of $K$ is determined by the images 
            of $\sqrt 2$, and $\sqrt 3$. 
            Show the possible images and conclude 
            that 
            \[
                \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \leq 4.  
            \]
            Construct all $4$ automorphisms and 
            conclude that  
            \[
                \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)
                \cong C_2 \times C_2.
            \]
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    We have that, for $f(x) = x^2 - 2 \in 
    \mathbb Q[x]$, 
    $\sqrt 2$ is a $0$. 
    This polynomial is monic, and furthermore 
    no lower degree nonzero polynomial can have 
    $\sqrt 2$ as a zero. If we suppose 
    that $f'$ is such a polynomial, then 
    \[
        f'(x) = x - a 
    \]
    such that $\sqrt 2 - a = 0$, meaning 
    $\sqrt 2 = a$, but then $f'(x)\not\in 
    \mathbb Q[x]$.
    Thus $f$ is the minimal polynomial of 
    $\sqrt 2$.
    Then 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2) : \mathbb Q] 
        = \deg f = 2.
    \]

    Suppose $\sqrt 3 \in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$. 
    Then, since $\sqrt 3 \not \in \mathbb Q$, we 
    have $\sqrt 3 \in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2) \setminus 
    \mathbb Q$.
    Then $\sqrt 3 = a + b\sqrt 2$ such that 
    $b \neq 0$. Then 
    \[
        3 = a^2 + 2ab\sqrt 2 + b^2. 
    \]
    If $a \neq 0$ we have 
    \[
        3 - a^2 - b^2 = 2ab\sqrt 2, 
    \]
    but the left-hand-side is rational and 
    the right-hand side is not, a contradiction. 
    Suppose $a = 0$. Then 
    \[
        3 = b^2 
    \]
    so $b = \sqrt 3 \not\in \mathbb Q$. 
    Thus $\sqrt 3 \not\in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$.
    By a similar argument as earlier, the minimal 
    polynomial of $\sqrt 3$ over $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$ is 
    $x^2 - 3$. 
    Thus, by the tower law, we have 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3) : \mathbb Q] = 2 \cdot 2 = 4. 
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    Let $L$ be the splitting field of $f$. 
    It is trivial to see that 
    \[
        f(\pm\sqrt 2) = f(\pm\sqrt 3) = 0
    \]
    are the zeros of $f$ in $\mathbb C \supset 
    \overline{\mathbb Q}$.  
    Therefore $L \subseteq K$.
    The basis of $K$ is $\{1,\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt 6\}$, 
    and so the first $3$ are contained in $L$ and furthermore 
    \[
        \sqrt 6 = \frac{3\sqrt 2}{\sqrt 3} \in L.
    \]
    Therefore $K = L$. 

    $L = K$ is algebraic over $\mathbb Q$ as 
    it contains $\mathbb Q$ and elements which 
    are the zeros of $f \in \mathbb Q[x]$. 
    Furthermore since $L=K$ is the splitting field 
    of a polynomial in $\mathbb Q[x]$ it is a 
    normal extension. Lastly since 
    $\mathbb Q$ is characteristic $0$, 
    we have that all algebraic extensions are 
    separable. 

    All three properties give us that 
    $L=K$ is Galois as an extension of 
    $\mathbb Q$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    The $\mathbb Q$-autmorphisms of $K$ are 
    those which fix $\mathbb Q$ and 
    preserve 
    \[
        f(a) = 0
    \]
    iff 
    \[
        f(\psi(a)) = 0. 
    \]
    We require 
    \[
        (x^2 - 2)(\sqrt 2) = 0
    \]
    so $\sqrt 2 \mapsto \pm 2$ since 
    \[
        (x^2 - 2)(a) = 0
    \]
    if $a \in \{\sqrt 2, -\sqrt 2\}$. 
    Similarly for $\sqrt 3 \mapsto \pm \sqrt 3$. 

    Then we have a maximum of $4$ possible 
    isomorphisms from $K \to K$ which 
    respect this. 


    There are two choices to make 
    with two options each. For each 
    valid $\mathbb Q$-automorphism we have 
    send each of $\sqrt 2$ and $\sqrt 3$ 
    to themselves or their additive inverse. 
    Thus $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) \cong C_2 \times C_2$. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $G$ be a finite group and let 
    $H \leq G$ be a subgroup of index 
    $n$. Let 
    \[
        X = G / H 
    \]
    be the set of left cosets of $H$.

    Define an action on $X$ by 
    \[
        g \cdot (aH) = (ga) H. 
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Prove carefully that this is a 
            group action. 
        \item Let $\phi : G \to S_x$ 
            be the homomorphism induced by 
            this action. 
            Prove that 
            \[
                \ker \phi = 
                \bigcap_{g\in G} gHg^{-1}.
            \]
            This subgroup is often called the 
            core of $H$ in $G$.
        \item Prove that $\ker \phi$ is the 
            largest normal subgroup of $G$ 
            contained in $H$.
        \item Assume now that 
            \[
                |G| = 21
            \]
            and that $H \leq G$ has 
            order $7$. 

            Use the action to prove that 
            $H \trianglelefteq G$. 
        \item Use Cauchy's theorem to prove that 
            every group of order $21$ has 
            a normal subgroup of order $7$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    We must prove the following: 
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item if $aH = bH$, then 
            $(ga)H = (gb)H$,
        \item $e \cdot (aH) = aH$, 
        \item $g_1 \cdot (g_2 \cdot H) = (g_1g_2) \cdot H$.
    \end{enumerate}
    We begin with the first.

    Suppose $aH = bH$. We defined the 
    action to be $g \cdot (aH) = (ga)H$ so 
    \begin{align*}
        aH &= bH \\ 
        g(aH) &= g(bH) \\ 
        (ga)H &= (gb)H.
    \end{align*}
    
    Next the second property: 
    \begin{align*}
        e \cdot aH 
        &= (ea)H \\ 
        &= (a)H \\ 
        &= aH.
    \end{align*}

    The last property is also evident from 
    the definition 
    \[
        g_1 (g_2 H) \underset{\text{def}}{=} (g_1g_2)H.
    \]
    
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    The homomorphism induced by $G$ 
    acting on $X$ is $\psi : G \to \operatorname{Sym}(X)$, 
    defined by 
    \[
        \psi(g)(aH) = g \cdot aH = (ga)H. 
    \]

    We want to find 
    \[
        \ker \phi = \{g \in G : \psi(g) = e\}.
    \]
    That is, we want to find all $g \in G$ such that 
    $\psi(g)(aH) = aH$ for every $aH \in X$.

    We remark that 
    \[
        aH = bH \Leftrightarrow b^{-1}a \in H, 
    \]
    and we need to find $g \in G$ such that 
    \[
        gxH = xH, \ \forall x \in G.
    \]
    Then we are looking for $g \in G$ such that 
    \[
        x^{-1}gx \in H
    \]
    for every $x \in G$. 

    This is equivalent to 
    \[
        g \in xHx^{-1} 
    \]
    for every $x \in G$. 

    Summing up all these equivalences we get 
    \[
        \ker \phi 
        = \bigcap_{g \in G} gHg^{-1}.
    \]

\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    The map $\psi$ is a group homomorphism. Then 
    $\ker \psi$ is a normal subgroup of the domain 
    $\ker \psi \trianglelefteq G$. 

    We have $\ker \psi = \bigcap_{g \in G} gHg^{-1}$ 
    from (b). For $g = e$ we have 
    \[
        \bigcap_{g\in G} \subseteq eHe^{-1} = H. 
    \]

    Let $N$ be some normal subgroup of $G$ such that 
    $N \subseteq H$. We must show that 
    $N \subseteq \ker \psi$. Since $N$ is invariant 
    under conjugation we have 
    \[
        N = gNg^{-1}
    \]
    for every $g \in G$.
    Since $N \subseteq H$, we have 
    \[
        N = gNg^{-1} \subseteq gHg^{-1}, \ \forall g \in G.
    \]
    Thus 
    \[
        N \subseteq \bigcap_{g \in G} gHg^{-1} 
        = \ker \psi.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    From Lagrange's theorem we have 
    \[
        |X| = \left|\frac{G}{H}\right|
        = \frac{|G|}{|H|} = 3. 
    \]
    The action induces the 
    homomorphism 
    $\psi : G \to S_3$. 

    Thus by the first isomorphism theorem 
    we have that 
    \[
        \frac{G}{\ker \psi} \cong \operatorname{im}
        \phi \leq S_3. 
    \]
    Thus 
    \[
        \left|\frac{G}{\ker \psi}\right| \leq 6. 
    \]
    We have $\ker \psi \subseteq H$, so 
    $|\ker \psi| \leq 7$. We must also have that 
    the order of the kernel divides $|G| = 21$. 
    We have $\ker \psi \geq 3$ by the constraint 
    of the quotient group having $\leq 6$ elements. 
    Then, $4,5,6$ do not divide $21$ so we must 
    have that $|\ker \psi| = 7$. By $(c)$, 
    $\ker \psi$ is the largest normal subgroup 
    of $G$ contained entirely in $H$, but 
    $|\ker \psi| = |H|$, so $H = \ker \psi$. 
    Thus $H$ is normal.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (e)]
    Cauchy's theorem tells us that if 
    $G$ is a finite group and $p$ is prime 
    such that $p$ divides $|G|$. Then 
    there exists an element in $G$ with 
    order $p$. Let $g$ be that element. Then
    $\langle g \rangle \leq G$. Apply this for 
    $p = 7$ so that we get 
    $|\langle g \rangle| = 7$. Apply (d) 
    to see that the kernel of the homomorphism 
    induced by the group action on the set of 
    cosets of $\langle g \rangle$ to see that 
    $\langle g \rangle$ is normal. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        R = \mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z
    \]
    with componentwise addition and multiplication. 
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Find all units in $R$. 
        \item Find all zero divisors in $R$. 
        \item Prove that every ideal $I \trianglelefteq R$ 
            has the form 
            \[
                I = m\mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z
            \]   
            for some integers $m,n \geq 0$. 
        \item Let 
            \[
                J = 2\mathbb Z \times 3 \mathbb Z.
            \]
            Identify the quotient 
            \[
                \frac{R}{J}
            \]
            up to isomorphism. 
        \item Is $J$ prime, maximal? 
        \item Find all pairs $(m,n)$ such that 
            \[
                m\mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z
            \]
            is a prime ideal of $R$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    We want to find $(a,b) \in R$ for which there 
    exists $(d,c) \in R$ such that 
    \[
        (a,b)(d,c) = (1,1),
    \]
    meaning 
    \[
        ad = 1, \ \text{and} \ bc = 1. 
    \]
    Then $a^{-1} = d$ and $b^{-1} = c$ in 
    $\mathbb Z$. The only units in 
    $\mathbb Z$ are $1$ and $-1$ both having 
    themselves as inverses. Thus 
    the units are $(a,b) = (1,1), (a,b) = (1,-1), 
    (a,b) = (-1, 1), (a,b) = (-1,-1)$ with 
    themselves as the multiplicative inverse 
    $(a,b)^{-1} = (c,d) = (a,b)$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    We want to find nonzero 
    $(a,b)$ for which there exists 
    nonzero $(c,d)$ such that 
    \[
        (a,b)(c,d) = (0,0).
    \]
    Therefore $(0,0)$ is out of the question. 
    We then want to find $a,b,c,d \in \mathbb Z$ such 
    that 
    \[
        ab = 0 \ \text{and} \ cd = 0, 
    \]
    but we forbid $a = b = 0$ or $c = d = 0$ 
    similtaneously. 
    Thus we can choose e.g. $a, d \in \mathbb Z\setminus\{0\}$, 
    $b = c = 0$ and vice-versa. 

    Then the zero-divsors are all pairs 
    \[
        (a,b),(c,d) \ \text{s.t.} \ 
        a,d \in \mathbb Z \setminus \{0\} \ 
        \text{and} \ b = c = 0 \ \text{or} \ 
        a = d = 0 \ \text{and} \ 
        b,c \in \mathbb Z\setminus\{0\}.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    It is obvious from the problem statement that 
    we are talking about nontrivial proper ideals. 

    We will first prove, for arbitrary 
    $m,n \in \mathbb Z$, that 
    $m\mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z$ is 
    an ideal (two-sided, though all ideals of 
    $R$ are two-sided since it is commutative) of 
    $R$. 

    $m\mathbb Z\times n\mathbb Z$ is the 
    direct product of the additive groups 
    $m\mathbb Z, n \mathbb Z$ and is therefore 
    itself an additive group. It is 
    a subgroup of $\mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$. 

    Suppose $(a,b) \in m \mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z$ 
    and that $(c,d) \in R$. Then $(a,b) = (m\alpha, n\beta)$ for 
    some $\alpha,\beta \in \mathbb Z$. 
    Then 
    \[
        (c,d)(a,b) = (c \cdot m \alpha,d \cdot n \beta) 
        = (m (c\alpha), n (d\beta)) \in m\mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z, 
    \]
    and likewise for right-multiplication.
    Thus $m \mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z$ is an ideal 
    of $R$ for arbitary $m,n$. 

    Suppose $I$ is an ideal of $R$. 
    Then $I$ is an additive subgroup of $R$ and 
    absorbs multiplication from $R$.

    The additive group $(R, +)$ is 
    $(\mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z, +)$, a (finitely generated) 
    abelian group. Thus every subgroup is a finitely generated 
    abelian group, meaning that for every subgroup $I \leq R$, we 
    have 
    \[
        I \cong \prod_{i=1}^k \mathbb Z_{p_i^{e_i}} \times 
        \prod_{i=1}^l \mathbb Z,
    \]
    by the fundamental theorem of finitely generated abelian 
    groups.
    From this it is clear that $I \cong m \mathbb Z \times n \mathbb Z$ 
    or $I \cong m \mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (d)]
    We recall that $\mathbb Z/n\mathbb Z \cong \mathbb Z_n$ and 
    thus immediately suspect 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Z\times \mathbb Z}{2\mathbb Z \times 3 \mathbb Z }
        \cong \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3.
    \]
    Define the homomorphism 
    $\phi : R \to \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3$ by 
    \[
        \phi(a,b) = (a \mod 2, b \mod 3). 
    \]
    Clearly $R$ is a homomorphism and 
    surjective. Thus $\operatorname{im}\phi = 
    \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3$. 
    Furthermore $\phi(a,b) = 0$ if and only if 
    $a \cong 0 \mod 2$ and $b \cong 0 \mod 3$, 
    meaning $a = 2x, b = 3y$ so 
    \[
        \ker \phi = 2\mathbb Z \times 3 \mathbb Z.
    \]
    By the first isomorphism theorem we have 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Z\times 
        \mathbb Z}{2\mathbb Z \times 3 \mathbb Z} = 
        \frac{R}{\ker \phi} \cong  
        \operatorname{im} \phi = 
        \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (e)]
    $R$ is a commutative ring so the 
    quotient ring is a field if and only if 
    the ideal is maximal. Similarly the 
    quotient is an integral domain if 
    and only if the quotient is a 
    prime ideal. 

    Thus we can see that the quotient is neither 
    since $(1,0)(0,1) = (0,0)$ in $\mathbb 
    Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_3$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (e)]
    Only $(m,n) \in \{(1,0),(1,p),(0,1),(p,1)\}$ 
    where $p$ are prime give us prime ideals. 
    Then the maximal ideals are a subset of this 
    and clearly they are those $(m,n) \in \{(p,1),(1,p)\}$.
    Up to isomorphism these sets are cut in half.
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Let $G$ be a finite abelian group with 
    $|G| = 72 = 2^3 \cdot 3^2$.
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item List all abelian groups of order 
            $|G|$ up to ismorphism.
        \item Which of the groups are cyclic?
        \item Let 
            \[
                A = (\mathbb Z_2)^3 \times 
                (\mathbb Z_3)^2.
            \]
            How many elements of order exactly 
            $6$ exist?
        \item Let 
            \[
                B = \mathbb Z_4 \times 
                \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_9.
            \]
            Find an element of order $36$ or 
            prove that no such elements exists. 
        \item Suppose $G$ is an abelian group 
            of order $72$ and contains an 
            element of order $72$. Prove 
            $G \cong \mathbb Z_{72}$.
            Suppose $G$ contains an element 
            of order $36$. Is $G$ cyclic or 
            can you find a counter-example?
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    We use a special case of the fundamental 
    theorem of finitely generated abelian groups, 
    that being the classification theorem for 
    finite abelian groups. We have 
    \[
        G \cong \prod_{i}\mathbb Z_{p_i^{e_i}}
    \] 
    such that 
    \[
        \prod_{i}p_i^{e_i} = 72. 
    \]
    We must then find the unique 
    ways of doing this. 
    We have $72 = 2^3 \cdot 3^2$. 
    Then we get the following groups 
    \begin{align*}
        (\mathbb Z_2)^3 \times (\mathbb Z_3)^2 \\ 
        \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_2^2 \times (\mathbb Z_3)^2 \\ 
        \mathbb Z_2^3 \times (\mathbb Z_3)^2 \\ 
        \mathbb Z_2^3 \times \mathbb Z_3^2 \\ 
        \mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z_2^2 \times \mathbb Z_3^2 \\ 
        (\mathbb Z_2)^3 \times \mathbb Z_3^2. 
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    Each $\mathbb Z_n$ is cyclic and the direct 
    product of such groups is cyclic if and only if 
    their orders are relatively prime. 

    That leaves only 
    \begin{align*}
        \mathbb Z_8 \times \mathbb Z_9. 
    \end{align*}

    This group is isomorphic to 
    $\mathbb Z_{72}$ which can be shown 
    with the first isomorphism theorem 
    using the surjective $\phi : \mathbb Z \to 
    \mathbb Z_8 \times \mathbb Z_9$, 
    \[
        \phi(x) = (x \mod 8, x \mod 9),
    \]
    and identifying that $\ker \phi = \mathbb Z_{72}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    We notice that 
    $1$ has order $2$ in 
    $\mathbb Z_2$ and 
    $1$ and $2$ have order $3$ in 
    $\mathbb Z_3$. Then 
    in the direct product the order is 
    the least common multiple of the 
    entries. Thus 
    \[
        (a,b,c,d,e)
    \]
    has order $6$ when at least one 
    of $a,b,c$ is non-zero and at least 
    one of $d,e$ is non-zero. 
    Then we can make $2$ choices for 
    $a,b,c$ since one must be non-zero 
    and $1$ choice for $d,e$ for 
    the same reason. Thus there are 
    $2^2 \times 3 = 12$ such elements. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    We have $1$ having 
    order $n$ in $\mathbb Z_n$. 
    Therefore $(1,1,1)$ has order 
    $\operatorname{lcm}(2,4,9) = 36$ in $B$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (e)]
    We begin with the second claim. 
    $B$ as defined in (d) has an element of 
    order $36$ and is not cyclic. 

    It is evident that $\mathbb Z_{72}$ has an 
    element of order $72$, namely $1$. 

    If $|G| = 72$ and there exists 
    $g \in G$ such that $|g| = 72$ then 
    $
        \langle g \rangle
    $
    is a subgroup of $G$ with order $72$, 
    meaning 
    \[
        \langle g \rangle 
        \cong C_{72} \cong \mathbb Z_{72}. 
    \]
    The only subgroup of $G$ with order $|G|$, 
    is $G$ so $\langle g \rangle = G$. 
    Thus 
    \[
        G \cong \mathbb Z_{72}.
    \]
      
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $E / \mathbb Q$ be a finite Galois extension such 
    that 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q) \cong S_5. 
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        G = \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q).
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Compute $[E:\mathbb Q]$.
        \item Prove that there exists an 
            intermediate field $K$ so that 
            \[
                [K : \mathbb Q] = 24. 
            \]
        \item Prove that there exists an intermediate 
            field $L$ such that  
            \[
                [L : \mathbb Q] = 10.
            \]
        \item Does there necessarily exist an intermediate field 
            $M$ such that 
            \[
                [M : \mathbb Q] = 7? 
            \]
        \item Let $A_5 \leq S_5$. Let $K = E^{A_5}$, the fixed 
            field of $A_5$.
            Compute 
            \[
                [K : \mathbb Q]
            \]
            and 
            \[
                [E:K].
            \]  
            Decide whether $K / \mathbb Q$ is Galois.
        \item Let 
            \[
                H = (\langle 1 \ 2 \ 3 \ 4 \ 5 )\rangle \leq S_5. 
            \]
            Let $K = E^H$. 
            Compute 
            \[
                [K : \mathbb Q]
            \]
            and 
            \[
                [E : K].
            \]
            Is $K / \mathbb Q$ necessarily Galois?
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    The extension $E/\mathbb Q$ is Galois. 
    Then 
    \[
        |\operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q)| = 
        [E : \mathbb Q]. 
    \]
    Thus 
    \[
        [E : \mathbb Q] 
        = |S_5| = 120.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    We have $H:=\langle (1 \ 2 \ 3 \ 4 \ 5)\rangle \leq S_5$ and 
    $|H| = 5$. By the tower law: 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q] = 24
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    We need a subgroup of order $12$. 
    Take $H := A_4$.

    Let $L = E^{A_4}$. Then 
    \[
        [L :\mathbb Q] = [S_5 : S_4] = 10.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    Suppose there exists 
    $M \subset E$ so that 
    $[M : \mathbb Q] = 7$. Then 
    $M / \mathbb Q$ is Galois and the 
    Galois group $H$ satisfies 
    $H \leq G$. Then 
    \[
        120 = |G| = [G : H] \cdot 7,
    \]
    meaning that $7$ would divide 
    $120$, which it does not. 
    Therefore there does not exist such an 
    intermediary field. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        E = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt 5).
    \]
    For $a,b,c \in \{0,1\}$, every element of $E$ 
    can be written uniquely as a $\mathbb Q$-linear 
    combination of $(\sqrt 2)^a, (\sqrt 3)^b, (\sqrt 5)^c$. 
    The basis is 
    \[
        \{1,\sqrt 2, \sqrt 3, \sqrt 5, \sqrt 6, \sqrt 10, \sqrt 15, \sqrt 30\}.
    \]
    Define automorphisms $\sigma_2,\sigma_3,\sigma_5\in 
    \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q)$ by 
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma_2(\sqrt2)=-\sqrt2,\qquad \sigma_2(\sqrt3)=\sqrt3,\qquad \sigma_2(\sqrt5)=\sqrt5, \\ 
        \sigma_3(\sqrt2)=\sqrt2,\qquad \sigma_3(\sqrt3)=-\sqrt3,\qquad \sigma_3(\sqrt5)=\sqrt5, \\ 
        \sigma_5(\sqrt2)=\sqrt2,\qquad \sigma_5(\sqrt3)=\sqrt3,\qquad \sigma_5(\sqrt5)=-\sqrt5.
    \end{align*}

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $[E:\mathbb Q] = 8$. 
            Then explain why 
            \[
                \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q) 
                \cong C_2 \times \cong C_2 \times \cong C_2.
            \]
        \item Let 
            \[
                H = \langle \sigma_2 \rangle.
            \]
            Find the fixed field 
            \[
                E^H.
            \]
        \item Let 
            \[
                H = \langle \sigma_2, \sigma_3 \rangle. 
            \]
            Find $E^H$.
        \item Let 
            \[
                H = \langle \sigma_2 \sigma_3\rangle. 
            \]
            Find $E^H$.
        \item Let 
            \[
                H = \langle \sigma_2\sigma_3, \sigma_5 \rangle.
            \]
            Find $E^H$.
            Compute the degrees 
            \[
                [E:E^H], [E^H : \mathbb Q].
            \]
        \item Let $K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 30)$. Find 
            $\operatorname{Gal}(E/K)$. 
        \item Let $K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2 + \sqrt 3)$. 
            Find $\operatorname{Gal}(E/K)$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    The problem states that every element of $E$ can be uniquely written as a $\mathbb Q$-linear combination of the $8$-element basis 
    \[
        \{1, \sqrt{2}, \sqrt{3}, \sqrt{5}, \sqrt{6}, \sqrt{10}, \sqrt{15}, \sqrt{30}\}.
    \]
    Since the dimension of $E$ as a vector space over $\mathbb Q$ is equal to the cardinality of its basis, we immediately obtain $[E:\mathbb Q] = 8$.

    Because $E$ is the splitting field of the separable polynomial $(x^2-2)(x^2-3)(x^2-5)$ over $\mathbb Q$, the extension is Galois, which implies $|\operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q)| = [E:\mathbb Q] = 8$. 
    
    The given automorphisms $\sigma_2, \sigma_3, \sigma_5$ satisfy $\sigma_i^2 = \text{id}$ for each $i \in \{2, 3, 5\}$ because applying any of them twice negates the respective radical twice (e.g., $\sigma_2(\sigma_2(\sqrt{2})) = \sigma_2(-\sqrt{2}) = \sqrt{2}$). Furthermore, these automorphisms commute with each other. Since $\operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q)$ is an abelian group of order $8$ where every non-identity element has order $2$, it must be isomorphic to the elementary abelian group of order $8$:
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q) \cong C_2 \times C_2 \times C_2.
    \]
\end{proof}
    
\begin{proof}[Solution to (b)]
    The subgroup $H$ is generated by $\sigma_2$, which sends $\sqrt{2} \mapsto -\sqrt{2}$ while fixing $\sqrt{3}$ and $\sqrt{5}$. An element $x \in E$ written in the standard basis is fixed by $\sigma_2$ if and only if the coefficients of all basis elements containing $\sqrt{2}$ (namely $\sqrt{2}, \sqrt{6}, \sqrt{10}, \sqrt{30}$) are zero. The remaining elements are generated by $\sqrt{3}$ and $\sqrt{5}$. Thus, the fixed field is:
    \[
        E^H = \mathbb Q(\sqrt{3}, \sqrt{5}).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (c)]
    An element is fixed by $H$ if it is simultaneously fixed by $\sigma_2$ and $\sigma_3$. 
    Being fixed by $\sigma_2$ eliminates coefficients for base components containing $\sqrt{2}$, while being fixed by $\sigma_3$ eliminates coefficients for base components containing $\sqrt{3}$. The only remaining basis elements unaffected by both negations are $1$ and $\sqrt{5}$. Thus, the fixed field is:
    \[
        E^H = \mathbb Q(\sqrt{5}).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (d)]
    The generator acts on the primary radicals as follows:
    \[
        \sigma_2\sigma_3(\sqrt{2}) = -\sqrt{2}, \quad \sigma_2\sigma_3(\sqrt{3}) = -\sqrt{3}, \quad \sigma_2\sigma_3(\sqrt{5}) = \sqrt{5}.
    \]
    Evaluating its effect on the product elements of the basis yields:
    \[
        \sigma_2\sigma_3(\sqrt{6}) = (-\sqrt{2})(-\sqrt{3}) = \sqrt{6}.
    \]
    Therefore, the basis elements explicitly fixed by $\sigma_2\sigma_3$ are $1$, $\sqrt{5}$, $\sqrt{6}$, and $\sqrt{30}$ (since $\sqrt{30} = \sqrt{5}\sqrt{6}$). Thus, the fixed field is:
    \[
        E^H = \mathbb Q(\sqrt{6}, \sqrt{5}).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (e)]
    From part (d), we know $\sigma_2\sigma_3$ fixes $\mathbb Q(\sqrt{6}, \sqrt{5})$. Now we apply the second generator $\sigma_5$, which maps $\sqrt{5} \mapsto -\sqrt{5}$ and fixes $\sqrt{6}$. The only basis components from part (d) fixed by $\sigma_5$ are $1$ and $\sqrt{6}$. Thus, the fixed field is:
    \[
        E^H = \mathbb Q(\sqrt{6}).
    \]
    By the Fundamental Theorem of Galois Theory, the degree of the extension over the fixed field equals the order of the subgroup:
    \[
        [E:E^H] = |H| = 4.
    \]
    By the tower law property of extension degrees ($[E:\mathbb Q] = [E:E^H][E^H:\mathbb Q]$):
    \[
        [E^H:\mathbb Q] = \frac{8}{4} = 2.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (f)]
        By Galois correspondence, $\operatorname{Gal}(E/K)$ consists of the automorphisms in $\operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q)$ that fix $K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt{30})$ elementwise. We test how the basic automorphisms act on $\sqrt{30}$:
        \begin{align*}
            \sigma_2(\sqrt{30}) &= (-\sqrt{2})\sqrt{3}\sqrt{5} = -\sqrt{30}, \\
            \sigma_3(\sqrt{30}) &= \sqrt{2}(-\sqrt{3})\sqrt{5} = -\sqrt{30}, \\
            \sigma_5(\sqrt{30}) &= \sqrt{2}\sqrt{3}(-\sqrt{5}) = -\sqrt{30}.
        \end{align*}
        Products of pairs of these maps will resolve the double negation: $\sigma_2\sigma_3(\sqrt{30}) = \sqrt{30}$, $\sigma_2\sigma_5(\sqrt{30}) = \sqrt{30}$, and $\sigma_3\sigma_5(\sqrt{30}) = \sqrt{30}$. Since $[E:K] = 4$, the subgroup must have order $4$. Thus:
        \[
            \operatorname{Gal}(E/K) = \{\text{id}, \sigma_2\sigma_3, \sigma_2\sigma_5, \sigma_3\sigma_5\}.
        \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution to (g)]
    We find which non-identity automorphisms fix $\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}$:
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma_2(\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}) &= -\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3} \neq \sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}, \\
        \sigma_3(\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}) &= \sqrt{2} - \sqrt{3} \neq \sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}, \\
        \sigma_5(\sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}) &= \sqrt{2} + \sqrt{3}.
    \end{align*}
    Since $\sigma_5$ fixes the generator of $K$, and $[E:K] = 2$ (as $K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt{2}, \sqrt{3})$ has degree $4$ over $\mathbb Q$), the Galois group must have order $2$. Therefore:
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(E/K) = \langle \sigma_5 \rangle = \{\text{id}, \sigma_5\}.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 5). 
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Show that $K$ is the splitting field of 
            \[
                f(x) = (x^2 - 2)(x^2-5)
            \]
            over $\mathbb Q$, and prove that 
            \[
                [K:\mathbb Q] = 4. 
            \]
        \item Find $G = \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q)$. 
            Describe it's elements explicitly by their 
            action on $\sqrt 2$ and $\sqrt 5$, and prove 
            that 
            \[
                G \cong C_2 \times C_2.
            \]
        \item List the subgroups of $G$.
        \item For each $H \leq G$, find the 
            fixed field $K^H$.
        \item Explain which intermediate extensions 
            are Galois over $\mathbb Q$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    We begin by finding the roots of 
    $f(x)$ in $\overline{\mathbb Q}$.

    $x^2 - 2$ has zeros 
    $\pm \sqrt 2$ and $x^2 - 5$ has zeros 
    $\pm \sqrt 5$.
    These are contained in $K$. 
    Thus the splitting field 
    $L$ is a subset $L \subseteq K$. 

    Now, $K$ has $\{1,\sqrt 2, \sqrt 5, \sqrt{10}\}$ 
    as the basis as a vector space over 
    $\mathbb Q$. The first $3$ elements are 
    in the splitting field $L$ and $\sqrt{10} = \sqrt 2 \sqrt 5 \in L$ 
    so since every elements of $K$ is a linear combination 
    of these elements which are indeed contained in 
    the splitting field we have $K \subseteq L$. 
    Thus $K = L$.

    We claimed that $B = \{1,\sqrt 2, \sqrt 5, \sqrt{10}\}$ 
    is a basis for the extension and then 
    \[
        |B| = [K : \mathbb Q], 
    \]
    but we did this without proof. 
    We proceed with the tower law instead 
    and observe that as $x^2 - 2$ is the minimal 
    polynomial of $\sqrt 2$ over $\mathbb Q$ we have 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2) : \mathbb Q] = \deg (x^2 - 2) = 2. 
    \]
    We observe that $\sqrt 5 \not\in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$, as 
    if this were the case we would have 
    $\sqrt 5 = a + b\sqrt 2$. If $b = 0$ then 
    we would have $\sqrt 5 \in \mathbb Q$ which 
    we do not. Working out the rest of the 
    cases we get contradictions where $\sqrt 2$ 
    is asserted to be rational which it is not. 
    Then $x^2 - 5$ is the minimal polynomial 
    of $\sqrt 5$ over $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$ meaning 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)] = 2 
    \]
    as before and using the tower law we get 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q] = 4.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution and proof (b)]
    $G \leq S_4$ since $K$ is a splitting 
    field of some polynomial of degree $4$.  

    Specifically $G$ is the group of 
    automorphisms on $K$ which fix $\mathbb Q$ 
    and preserve 
    \[
        \psi(\sqrt 2)^2 - 2 = 0
    \]
    as with the minimal polynomial of 
    $\sqrt 5$. Thus we get 
    $\psi(\sqrt 2) = \pm \sqrt 2$ and 
    $\psi(\sqrt 5) = \pm \sqrt 5$ for 
    every $\psi \in G$.
    Thus, for each $\psi \in G$ we make 
    the choice of whether to flip the sign 
    of each root or not, meaning 
    $G \cong C_2 \times C_2$. 
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution of (c)]
    The nontrivial subgroups of $G$ are those which 
    one root is fixed, i.e. 
    \[
        \{0\} \times C_2, \ 
        C_2 \times \{0\}.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Solution of (d)]
    For each $\psi \in G$ we have 
    \[
        \psi[\mathbb Q] = \mathbb Q.
    \]
    If $\psi$ fixes a basiselement $\alpha$ of 
    $K$, then $\psi$ will fix $\mathbb Q(\alpha)$. 
    Suppose $\alpha$ is a basiselement and 
    $\psi(\alpha) = \alpha$. Then 
    for each $a + b\alpha \in \mathbb Q(\alpha)$, we have 
    (since $\psi$ is a homomorphism), 
    \[
        \psi(a + b\alpha) 
        = \psi(a) + \psi(b)\psi(\alpha) 
        = a + b\alpha.
    \]
    Thus $\psi$ fixes $\mathbb Q(\alpha)$. 

    Then the fixed fields are 
    \[
        K, \ \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2), \
        \mathbb Q(\sqrt 5), \
        \mathbb Q(\sqrt{10}), \
        \mathbb Q.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (e)]
    $G \cong C_2 \times C_2$ is abelian, hence 
    every subgroup is normal. Thus every 
    intermediate extension is Galois.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Work over $\mathbb F_2$. Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 + x + 1, 
        g(x) = x^3 + x^2 + 1
    \]
    in $\mathbb F_2[x]$.

    Let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle, \
        L = \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle g \rangle.
    \]

    Write 
    \[
        \alpha = x + \langle f \rangle \in K, 
        \ \beta = x + \langle g \rangle \in L.
    \]

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Show that $f$ and $g$ are irreducible over 
            $\mathbb F_2$.
            Conclude that they are fields. 
        \item Show that $K \cong L$ as fields. 
        \item Does the assignment $\alpha \mapsto \beta$ 
            necessarily define a $\mathbb F_2$-field homomorphism 
            \[
                K \to L? 
            \]
            Decide in this specific case. That is, check whether 
            there exists an $\mathbb F_2$-field homomorphism 
            $\phi : K \to L$ such that 
            \[
                \phi(\alpha) = \beta. 
            \]
        \item Find an explicit element of $L$ to which 
            $\alpha$ can be sent. In other words, find 
            $\gamma \in L$ such that 
            \[
                f(\gamma) = 0.
            \]
            Then explain why $\alpha \mapsto \gamma$ does 
            define a $\mathbb F_2$-field homomorphism $K \to L$.
        \item Let 
            \[
                \sigma : K \to K, \ \sigma(a) = a^2.
            \]
            Compute 
            \[
                \sigma(\alpha), \ \sigma^2(\alpha), \ 
                \sigma^3(\alpha).
            \]
            Then determine $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2)$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (a)]
    Both $f,g$ have degree $3 \in \{2,3\}$ and are 
    thus irreducible over $\mathbb F_2$ if and only if they 
    have no zeros in $\mathbb F_2$.

    We quickly check that 
    \[
        f(0) = f(1) = g(0) = g(1) = 1.
    \]

    Since $\mathbb F_2$ is a field, the polynomial ring (in 
    one variable) is a 
    PID and thus a commutative ring. Therefore the 
    quotient ring is a field if and only if 
    the ideal we quotient by is maximal. 
    As $\mathbb F_2[x]$ is a PID we have that 
    the ideals are maximal if and only if they are prime. 
    Thus irreducibility implies the ideal is maximal which 
    in turn implies the quotient ring is a field. 

    Therefore we conclude that $\mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle$ 
    and $\mathbb F_2[x]/\langle g \rangle$ are fields. 
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    We have that $f,g$ are irreducible and there is a standard 
    result which says that in this case 
    \[
        \left|\frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle f \rangle}\right|
        = |\mathbb F_2|^{\deg f} 
        = |\mathbb F_2|^{\deg g} = 
        \left|\frac{\mathbb F_2[x]}{\langle g \rangle}\right|
    \]
    and from here we can apply the classification of finite fields 
    to conclude that both quotient rings are then isomorphic.

    For the $|\mathbb F_2|^{\deg f}$ part we can elucidate the 
    claim as follows. We will do this for the quotient with 
    $\langle f \rangle$, and the approach is the same for 
    $g$. 

    Suppose $l(x) \in \mathbb F_2[x]$. Then 
    $l(x) = f(x)q(x) + r(x)$ where $0 \leq \deg r(x) < \deg f$. 
    Therefore each element in the quotient can be written 
    as a linear combination of $1, x, x^2$ with scalars in 
    $\mathbb F_2$, since the $f(x)q(x)$ term vanishes. 
    Thus there are $2^3$ choices. 
\end{proof}

\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    Suppose $\phi : K \to L$ is a map such that 
    $\phi(\alpha) = \beta$. 
    The question is then if 
    \[
        f(\phi(\alpha)) = 0. 
    \]
    We have 
    \[
        f(\beta) = \beta^3 + \beta + 1,
    \]
    and
    \[
        \beta^3 = \beta^2 + 1 
    \]
    so the question is whether 
    \[
        \beta(\beta + 1) = 0.  
    \]
    This cannot be since $L$ is a field and 
    this would imply $\beta = 1$ or 
    $\beta = 0$, neither of which can be the case. 

    Thus $\alpha \mapsto \beta$ does not 
    constitute a $\mathbb F_2$-fixing homomorphism from $K \to L$.
     
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (d)]
    We need $\gamma \in L$ such that 
    $f(\gamma) = 0$.

    Let $\gamma := \beta^3$. 
    Then 
    \[
        f(\gamma) 
        = f(\beta^3) 
        = \beta^2 + (\beta^2 + 1) + 1 
        = 0.
    \]
    Define $\Phi : K \to L$ by 
    \[
        \Phi(h(\alpha)) = h(\gamma)
    \]
    for $h \in \mathbb F_2[x]$.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (e)]
    We have 
    \[
        \sigma(\alpha) = \alpha^2.
    \]
    Then 
    \[
        \sigma^2(\alpha) = 
        \sigma(\alpha^2) = \alpha^4  
        = \alpha^2 + \alpha.
    \]
    Lastly 
    \[
        \sigma^3(\alpha) 
        = \sigma(\alpha^2 + \alpha) 
        = \alpha^4 + \alpha^2 
        = \alpha. 
    \]

    We always have that 
    $\mathbb F_{q^n} / \mathbb F_{q}$ is Galois 
    with $[\mathbb F_{q^n} : \mathbb F_q] = n$, 
    and furthermore the Galois group is 
    always the cyclic group of order $n$ generated 
    by the Frobenious automorphism $a \mapsto a^q$. 
    Thus 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2) 
        = C_3 = \langle \sigma \rangle.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        G = \mathbb Z_{12} \times \mathbb Z_8.
    \]
    Let 
    \[
        H = \langle (4,2) \rangle \leq G.
    \]
    We want to determine $G/H$ up to isomorphism.

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Find the order of $(4,2) \in 
            \mathbb Z_{12} \times \mathbb Z_8$. 
            Conclude $|H|$ and compute 
            \[
                |G/H|.
            \]
        \item Show that in $G/H$, we have the relation 
            \[
                4 \overline{e_1} + 2 \overline{e_2} = 0, 
            \]
            where 
            \[
                \overline{e_1} = (1,0) + H, 
                \ \overline{e_2} = (0,1) + H.
            \]
            Record the original relations so 
            $G/H$ is generated by $\overline{e_1},\overline{e_2}$ 
            with $12\overline{e_1} = 0, 8 \overline{e_2} = 0$ and 
            the above relation.
        \item Prove that $G/H$ is not cyclic.
        \item Conclude that $G/H \cong \mathbb Z_4 \times 
            \mathbb Z_2$.
        \item Construct a surjective homomorphism 
            \[
                \phi : \mathbb Z_{12} \times 
                \mathbb Z_8 \to \mathbb Z_4 \times \mathbb Z_2 
            \]
            such that 
            \[
                \ker \phi = H.
            \]
            Use the first isomorphism theorem to conclude 
            the previous result.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}

\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    We have that $4$ has order $3$ in $\mathbb Z_{12}$ and 
    $2$ has the order $4$ in $\mathbb Z_8$.
    Then $|(4,2)| = \operatorname{lcm}(3,4) = 12$. 
    Thus $|H| = 12$. 

    Then, by Lagrange's theorem we have 
    \[
        |G/H| = 12 \cdot 8 / 12 = 8.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (b)]
    Because LaTeX is annoying we will denote 
    $\overline{e_1} =: e_1$ and likewise for $\overline{e_2}$. 
    We have $H = \langle 4,2 \rangle$ so 
    $(4,2) H = H$, equivalently $(4,2) \in H$, equivalently 
    $(4,2)H = 0$ in the quotient. Then, since $(4,2)H = 
    4e_1 + 2e_2$ we see that the relation holds.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (c)]
    If $G/H$ was cyclic it must contain some element of 
    order $|G/H| = 8$. Suppose $(a,b)H \in G/H$.  
    Then $(a,b) = ae_1 + be_2$. Then it is evident that 
    $(a,b)$ cannot have order $8$ since we already have the 
    relation given in (b).
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (d)]
    By the classification theorem for finite abelian groups we have 
    that the groups of order $|G/H| = 8$, up to isomorphism are 
    \[
        \prod_{i}\mathbb Z_{p_i^{e_i}}
    \]
    such that 
    \[
        \prod_{i} p_i^{e_i} = 8.
    \]
    These are $\mathbb Z_2^3, \ \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_4, \ 
    \mathbb Z_8$.
    
    We have that $G/H$ has no element of order $8$ hence 
    $\mathbb Z_8$ is out of the question. 
    Furthermore, $(1,1)$ has order $4$ so $\mathbb Z_2^3$ is also 
    out of the question. Therefore 
    \[
        G \cong \mathbb Z_2 \times \mathbb Z_4.
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of (e)]
    Let $\phi : \mathbb Z_{12} \times \mathbb Z_8 
    \to \mathbb Z_4 \times \mathbb Z_2$, by 
    \[
        \phi(a,b) = (a \mod 4, b \mod 2). 
    \]
    Then this map is clearly surjective and 
    \[
        \ker \phi = H. 
    \]
    Thus, by the first isomorphism theorem we have the desired 
    result.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $G = D_4$ be the symmetry group of the square. It has 
    $8$ elements: four rotations and four reflections. 

    We color the four vertices using black and white. 
    Two colorings are considered equivalent if you can obtain 
    one from the other by a symmetry of the square. 

    Let $X$ be the set of all such colorings. 
    Then $|X| = 16$.

    The group $D_4$ acts on $X$ by permuting the vertices. 

    We want to count the number of different colorings up to 
    the equivalence class created by this action.

    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item State Burnside's formula. 
        \item Label the vertices $1,2,3,4$ cyclically. 
            List the cycle of each symmetry of the square 
            as the permutation across vertices. 
        \item For a permutation $g$ of the vertices
            prove that the number of colorings fixed by $g$ is 
            $2^{c(g)}$ where $c(g)$ is the number of disjoint cycles 
            in the cycle decomposition of $g$.
        \item Use Burnside's formula to compute the number of  
            essentially different colorings of the square. 
        \item Now color the square using $m$ colors instead of $2$. 
            Find a formula for the number of essentially different 
            colorings in terms of $m$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}

\begin{proof}[Solution of (a)]
    Burnside's formula is 
    \[
        \#(X/G) = \frac{1}{|G|}\sum_{g\in G}|X^g|,
    \]
    where  
    \[
        X^g = \{x \in X : g \cdot x = x\}.
    \]
    $X^g$ is the set of $x \in X$ such that $g$ fixes $x$, meaning 
    it is like the opposite of $\operatorname{Stab}_G(x)$. Instead 
    of the $g \in G$ which leave $x$ untouched, it is the set 
    of $x$ which $g \in G$ leave untouched.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Solution of (b)]
    Denote $r_{90}$ as rotating $90$ degrees, $r_{180}$ for 
    $180$, etc. 
    Then $r_{90}(1, 2, 3, 4) = (4,1,2,3)$ and 
    $r_{180}(1,2,3,4) = (3,4,1,2)$ and $r_{270}(1,2,3,4) 
    = (2,3,4,1)$. 

    For reflections we denote $s_{\uparrow}$ as a reflection 
    along vertical center-line, $s_{\rightarrow}$ as a reflection 
    along the horizontal, $s_{\nearrow}$ as one diagonal and 
    $s_{\searrow}$ as the other. 
    Then $s_{\uparrow}(1,2,3,4) = (3,4,2,1)$, 
    $s_{\rightarrow}(1,2,3,4) = (2,1,4,3)$, 
    $s_{\nearrow}(1,2,3,4) = (1,4,3,2)$ and 
    $s_{\searrow}(1,2,3,4) = (3,2,1,4)$.

    In cycle notation we get $r_{90} = (1 \ 2 \ 3 \ 4)$ and then 
    $r_{180} = r_{90}^2 = (1 \ 3)(2 \ 4)$ and similarly for $270$ we 
    get $r_{270} = r_{90} \circ r_{180} = (1 \ 4 \ 3 \ 2)$. 
    Then reflections are $s_{\uparrow} = (1 \ 4 \ 2 \ 3)$, 
    $s_{\rightarrow} = (1 \ 2)(3 \ 4)$, 
    $s_{\nearrow} = (2 \ 4)$ and $s_{\searrow} = (1 \ 3)$.
\end{proof}


\begin{prob}
    Let $R = \mathbb Z[x]$. 
    Define $\phi : R \to \mathbb F_3$ by 
    \[
        \phi(f) = f(1) \mod 3. 
    \]
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Prove that $\phi$ is a ring 
            homomorphism and find 
            \[
                \ker \phi.
            \]
        \item Use the first isormorphism theorem 
            to identify the quotient 
            $\mathbb Z[x]/\langle 3, x-1 \rangle$.
            Decide whether $\langle 3, x-1 \rangle$ is 
            prime or maximal.
        \item[] Now let $J = \langle 3, x^2 + 1 \rangle 
            \subseteq \mathbb Z[x]$.
        \item Show that 
            \[
                \mathbb Z[x]/J \cong 
                \mathbb F_3[x]/\langle x^2 + 1 \rangle.
            \]
            Decide whether $J$ is prime or maximal.
        \item[] Let 
            \[
                K = \mathbb F_3[x]/\langle x^2 + 1\rangle,
            \]
            and let 
            \[
                \alpha = x + \langle x^2 + 1\rangle \in K.
            \]
        \item Show that $K$ is a field with $9$ 
            elements and basis $\{1,\alpha\}$ 
            over $\mathbb F_3$.
            Compute $\alpha^2, \alpha^3, \alpha^4$ and 
            determine the order of $\alpha$ in $K^\times$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Proof of a few problems]
    We have 
    \begin{align*}
        \phi(f+g) 
        &= (f+g)(1) \mod 3 \\ 
        &= (f(1) \mod 3 + g(1) \mod 3) \mod 3 \\ 
        &= \phi(f) + \phi(g). 
    \end{align*} 
    Similarly, 
    \begin{align*}
        \phi(fg) 
        &= f(1)g(1) \mod 3 \\ 
        &= f(1) \mod 3 \cdot g(1) \mod 3 \\ 
        &= \phi(f)\phi(g).
    \end{align*}
    We can also check $\phi(1) = 1$ is trivial.

    We want to find those $f \in R$ such 
    that $\phi(f) = 0$. Equivalently those 
    $f$ for which $f \equiv 0 \mod 3$ or 
    those $f$ which are divisible by $x-1$ 
    as $f(x) = g(x)(x-1) = 0$ for $x = 1$. 
    Thus 
    \[
        \langle 3, x-1\rangle \subseteq \ker \phi.
    \]
    Suppose $f \in \ker \phi$. 
    Then $\phi(f) \equiv 0 \mod 3$ so $f$ is 
    divisible by $3$ or $x-1$ so 
    \[
        \ker \phi \subseteq \langle 3, x-1\rangle. 
    \]

    Moving on... 

    \medskip 

    $\phi$ is surjective. Consider some 
    $n \in \mathbb F_3$. Then $f(x) = n$ 
    is sent to $n$ by $\phi$. 
    Therefore, by the first isomorphism theorem, 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Z[x]}{\langle 3, x-1\rangle} 
        \cong \mathbb F_3.
    \]
    Since $\mathbb F_3$ is a field we conclude 
    that $\langle 3,x-1 \rangle$ is maximal, because 
    a commutative ring (which $\mathbb Z[x]$ is) quotiented 
    by an ideal is a field if and only if 
    the ideal is maximal. 

    Moving on... 

    \medskip

    We will compare $\langle x^2 + 1 \rangle$ as an 
    ideal of $\mathbb R[x]$ vs. $\mathbb C[x]$.

    It is well known that 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb R[x]}{\langle x^2 + 1 \rangle} 
        \cong \mathbb C 
    \]
    via the first isomorphism theorem applied to 
    the homomorphism $f \mapsto f(i)$.

    Over $\mathbb C$, $x^2 + 1$ splits 
    into $(x + i)(x-i)$ both of which 
    are polynomials in $\mathbb C[x]$ so 
    $\langle x^2 + 1 \rangle$ is not even 
    prime and thus $\mathbb C/\langle x^2 + 1 \rangle$ is 
    not an integral domain and hence ceirtainly not 
    isomorphic to $\mathbb C$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $G,H$ be finite groups and let 
    $\phi : G \to H$ be a group homomorphism.

    Prove that 
    \[
        \frac{|G|}{|\ker \phi|} 
    \]
    divides $H$.

    Then prove that if $\gcd(|G|,|H|) = 1$, then 
    $\phi$ is the trivial homomorphism
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    By the first isomorphism theorem/fundamental theorem 
    of group homomorphisms, we have 
    \[
        \frac{|G|}{|\ker \phi|} 
        = \left|\frac{G}{\ker \phi}\right| 
        = |\operatorname{im} \phi|. 
    \]
    We note that $\operatorname{im}\phi$ is a subgroup 
    of $H$ (this can be proven by observing 
    that $\phi(e_G) = e_H$, $\phi(a), \phi(b) \in H 
    \Rightarrow \phi(a)\phi(b) = \phi(ab) \in H$, and so on), 
    so by Lagrange's theorem we have 
    that $|\operatorname{im} \phi|$ divides 
    $|H|$, which by the previous equalities, 
    gives the desired result.

    Assume $\gcd(|G|,|H|) = 1$.
    Then $|G|$ does not divide $|H|$, but 
    \[
        \frac{|G|}{|\ker \phi|}
    \]
    does divide $|H|$.
    Then $|G|$ does not split into factors 
    $a,b$ such that either divides $|H|$.
    Since  
    \[
        |G| = |\ker \phi| \cdot |\operatorname{im} \phi|
    \]
    and $|\operatorname{im} \phi|$ does divide $|H|$, it must 
    be the case that one of these factors is $1$ 
    and the other is $|G|$. We cannot not 
    have $|\operatorname{im} \phi| = |G|$ by assumption. Hence 
    $|\ker \phi| = |G|$ and $\phi$ is then the 
    trivial homomorphism $g \mapsto e_H$ for ever 
    $g \in G$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    List all finite abelian groups of 
    order $45 = 3^2 \cdot 5$ up to 
    isomorphism. Which are cyclic?
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}[Solution]
    By the classification theorem for finite 
    abelian groups, which is a special case of the 
    fundamental theorem of finitely generated abelian 
    groups, we have 
    \[
        G \cong \prod_{i}\mathbb Z_{p_i^{e_i}}
    \]
    such that 
    \[
        \prod_{i} p_i^{e_i} = 45.
    \]
    Then the unique ways to factor $45$ gives 
    us 
    \begin{align*}
        \mathbb Z_{3}^2 \times \mathbb Z_5 \\ 
        \mathbb Z_9 \times \mathbb Z_5. 
    \end{align*}
    For 
    \[
        H = \prod_i \mathbb Z_{q_i}
    \]
    we have that $H$ is cyclic only if 
    all $q_i$ are relatively prime. 
    Thus $\mathbb Z_9 \times \mathbb Z_5$ is 
    the only one that is cyclic, and 
    we can quickly see that it is 
    indeed generated by $(1,1)$. 
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        \sigma = (1 \ 2 \ 5)(3 \ 4) \in S_5.
    \]
    Find the order of $\sigma$, decide whether 
    $\sigma \in A_5$ and decide whether 
    \[
        \langle \sigma \rangle \trianglelefteq 
        S_5.
    \]
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Since $\sigma$ is the composition of 
    two disjoint cycles of lengths $2$ and 
    $3$ respectively we have 
    $\operatorname{ord}(\sigma) = 
    \operatorname{lcm}(2,3) = 6$.

    We have that $(1 \ 2 \ 5) = (1 \ 2)(2 \ 5)$ so 
    \[
        \sigma = (1 \ 2)(2 \ 5)(3 \ 4) \not\in A_5.
    \]
    If $\sigma$ was an element of the alternating group 
    we would automatically rule out normality. 

    We have $\sigma^3 = (3 \ 4) \in \langle \sigma \rangle$ 
    and we do the all-or-nothing test and ask whether 
    the subgroup contains all transpositions in $S_5$. 
    In $S_5$ there are $\binom{5}{2} = 10$ transpositions 
    and the only pure transposition in the subgroup is 
    the afforementioned $(3 \ 4)$ and the subgroup is 
    therefore not normal.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $D_4$ act on $X$ the set of colorings 
    of the vertices of a square using $m$ colors.   

    Use Burnside's formula to show that the number of 
    distinct (up to the equivalence class generated by 
    the action) colorings is 
    \[
        \frac{m^4 + 2m^3 + 3m^2 + 2m}{8}.
    \]
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
Let the vertices of the square be labeled $1, 2, 3, 4$ in counterclockwise order. The dihedral group $D_4$ acts on the vertices as a subgroup of $S_4$, and this action induces an action on the set $X$ of all $m^4$ possible vertex colorings. 

By Burnside's Lemma, the number of distinct colorings up to symmetry (orbits) is given by:
\[
|X/D_4| = \frac{1}{|D_4|} \sum_{g \in D_4} |X^g|
\]
where $|D_4| = 8$, and $X^g$ denotes the set of colorings fixed by the group element $g$. A coloring is fixed by $g$ if and only if all vertices belonging to the same cycle in the disjoint cycle decomposition of $g$ are assigned the exact same color. If a permutation decomposes into $k$ disjoint cycles (including $1$-cycles), there are $m^k$ independent choices of colors, so $|X^g| = m^k$.

We analyze the $8$ elements of $D_4$ by their geometric actions and corresponding cycle structures:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{The Identity ($e$):} 
    The identity element leaves all vertices fixed. Its cycle structure is $(1)(2)(3)(4)$, which yields $k = 4$ disjoint cycles. 
    \[ |X^e| = m^4 \]

    \item \textbf{Rotations by $\pm 90^\circ$ ($r, r^3$):} 
    A rotation by $90^\circ$ maps $1 \to 2 \to 3 \to 4 \to 1$. Both $r$ and its inverse $r^3$ form a single $4$-cycle: $(1\,2\,3\,4)$ and $(1\,4\,3\,2)$ respectively. Each has $k = 1$ cycle, requiring all 4 vertices to share a single color.
    \[ |X^r| = |X^{r^3}| = m^1 = m \]

    \item \textbf{Rotation by $180^\circ$ ($r^2$):} 
    This rotation maps opposite vertices to each other ($1 \leftrightarrow 3$ and $2 \leftrightarrow 4$). Its cycle structure is $(1\,3)(2\,4)$, giving $k = 2$ disjoint cycles. Vertices $1$ and $3$ must share a color, and vertices $2$ and $4$ must share a color.
    \[ |X^{r^2}| = m^2 \]

    \item \textbf{Reflections across Diagonal Axes ($s, r^2s$):} 
    There are $2$ reflections whose axes pass through opposite pairs of vertices. 
    \begin{itemize}
        \item One reflection fixes vertices $1$ and $3$ while swapping $2 \leftrightarrow 4$, yielding the cycle type $(1)(3)(2\,4)$.
        \item The other reflection fixes vertices $2$ and $4$ while swapping $1 \leftrightarrow 3$, yielding the cycle type $(2)(4)(1\,3)$.
    \end{itemize}
    Both elements yield $k = 3$ disjoint cycles.
    \[ |X^s| = |X^{r^2s}| = m^3 \]

    \item \textbf{Reflections across Midpoint Axes ($rs, r^3s$):} 
    There are $2$ reflections whose axes pass through the midpoints of opposite edges. These reflections leave no vertices fixed, instead swapping adjacent pairs of vertices simultaneously ($1 \leftrightarrow 2$ and $3 \leftrightarrow 4$, or $1 \leftrightarrow 4$ and $2 \leftrightarrow 3$). Their cycle structures are of the form $(\bullet\,\bullet)(\bullet\,\bullet)$, yielding $k = 2$ disjoint cycles.
    \[ |X^{rs}| = |X^{r^3s}| = m^2 \]
\end{enumerate}

Summing the sizes of the fixed-point sets across all $8$ elements yields:
\begin{align*}
\sum_{g \in D_4} |X^g| &= |X^e| + |X^r| + |X^{r^3}| + |X^{r^2}| + |X^s| + |X^{r^2s}| + |X^{rs}| + |X^{r^3s}| \\
&= m^4 + m + m + m^2 + m^3 + m^3 + m^2 + m^2 \\
&= m^4 + 2m^3 + 3m^2 + 2m
\end{align*}

Applying Burnside's formula by dividing this sum by $|D_4| = 8$, we find that the total number of distinct colorings up to symmetry is:
\[
|X/D_4| = \frac{m^4 + 2m^3 + 3m^2 + 2m}{8}
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $R = \mathbb Z \times \mathbb Z$.
    Find all units and zero divisors in 
    $R$.
    Decide whether the ideal 
    \[
        \mathbb Z \times \{0\}
    \]
    is prime and/or maximal.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    The set of units in $\mathbb Z^2$ are 
    the $2$-combinations of units in 
    $\mathbb Z$, of which there are $1,-1$ 
    both of which are their own inverse. 
    Therefore the units of $\mathbb Z^2$ are 
    $(-1,-1), (-1,1), (1,-1), (1,1)$, all 
    with themselves as their multiplicative inverse. 

    Let $\phi : \mathbb Z^2 \to \mathbb Z$ by 
    \[
        \phi(a,b) = b. 
    \]
    Then $\phi$ is a surjection and $\ker \phi$ is 
    \[
        \ker \phi = \mathbb Z \times \{0\}.
    \]
    Then, by the first isomorphism theorem: 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Z^2}{\mathbb Z\times \{0\}} 
        \cong \mathbb Z. 
    \]
    $\mathbb Z$ is an integral domain so $\mathbb Z 
    \times \{0\}$ is prime, but not maximal as 
    $\mathbb Z$ is not a field. 
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Define 
    \[
        \phi : \mathbb Z[x] \to \mathbb Z_5
    \]
    by 
    \[
        \phi(f) = f(3) \mod 5.  
    \]
    Prove that 
    \[
        \ker \phi = \langle 5, x-3\rangle. 
    \]
    Then identify $\mathbb Z[x]/\ker \phi$ and 
    decide whether $\ker \phi$ is prime or 
    maximal.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    Suppose $f \in \ker \phi$. 
    Then $\phi (f) = 0$, equivalently 
    $f(3) \equiv 0$ in $\mathbb Z_5$.   
    Then $f(x) = q(x)(x-3)$ or 
    $f(x) = 5q(x)$ so 
    \[
        \ker \phi \subseteq \langle 5, x-3\rangle.
    \]
    Suppose $f(x) = q(x)(x-3)$. Then 
    $f(3) \equiv 0$, likewise if 
    $f(x) = 5q(x)$. Then 
    \[
        \langle 5,x-3 \rangle \subseteq \ker \phi.
    \]

    $\phi$ is surjective since, for $n \in \mathbb Z_5$ we have 
    $f(x) := n \Rightarrow \phi(n) \equiv n$. Thus by the first 
    isomorphism theorem: 
    \[
        \frac{\mathbb Z[x]}{\ker \phi} 
        \cong \mathbb Z_5.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 + x + 1 \in \mathbb F_2[x].
    \]
    Show that $f$ is irreducible over $\mathbb F_2$. 
    Let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle, \ 
        \alpha = x + \langle f \rangle. 
    \]
    Show that $K$ is a field with $8$ elements and 
    basis 
    \[
        \{1, \alpha, \alpha^2\} 
    \]
    over $\mathbb F_2$.

    Compute $\alpha^n$ for $3 \leq n \leq 7$.
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    $f$ is degree $3$ and is thus irreducible if and 
    only if it has no zeros in the ground-field. Checking: 
    \[
        f(0) = f(1) = 1
    \]
    we see that $f$ is irreducible over $\mathbb F_2$.

    Since $\mathbb F_2$ is a field we have that 
    the polynomial ring is a PID. Then, 
    $\langle f \rangle$ is maximal if and 
    only if it is prime. Thus, since all PID's 
    are commutative rings we get that 
    $K = \mathbb F_2[x]/\langle f \rangle$ is 
    a field if and only if $f$ is irreducible over 
    $\mathbb F_2$. Therefore $K$ is a field. 

    Now let $g(x) \in \mathbb F_2[x]$ so 
    $g(x) = q(x)f(x) + r(x)$ where 
    $0 \leq \deg r < \deg f$. 
    Then sinec $q(x)f(x)$ vanishes 
    over $\langle f \rangle$ we get that 
    $g$ is a linear combination of 
    $1,x,x^2$ with scalars in $\mathbb F_2$. 
    Thus there are $|\mathbb F_2|^{\deg f} = 2^3 = 8$ possibilities. 

    \begin{align*}
        \alpha^3 + \alpha + 1 
        &= 0 \\ 
        \alpha^3 
        &= \alpha + 1 \\ 
        \alpha^4 &= \alpha(\alpha + 1) = \alpha^2 + \alpha \\ 
        \alpha^5 &= \alpha(\alpha^2 + \alpha) = \alpha^2 + \alpha + 1 \\ 
        \alpha^6 &= \alpha^3 + \alpha^2 + \alpha = \alpha^2 + 1 \\ 
        \alpha^7 &= \alpha^3 + \alpha = \alpha + \alpha + 1 = 1.
    \end{align*}
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let $K$ be the field from the previous problem.
    Define $\sigma : K \to K$, $\sigma(a) = a^2$.

    Show that $\sigma$ is an automorphism. 
    Compute 
    \[
        \sigma(\alpha), \ \sigma^2(\alpha), \ \sigma^3(\alpha).
    \]
    Determine $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2)$.
\end{prob}

\begin{proof}
    We have $K \cong \mathbb F_8$ since $|K| = 8$ and 
    $K$ is a field and finite field are classified 
    up to isomorphism. 

    Let $\psi : \mathbb F_8 \to \mathbb F_8$ by 
    \[
        \psi(a) = a^2. 
    \]
    Then suppose 
    \[
        \psi(a) = 0. 
    \]
    Then $a = 0$ so $\ker \psi = \{0\}$ and 
    is thus injective and since the domain 
    equals the codomain and they are finite bijectivity 
    follows. Now we show that this is indeed a homomorphism: 
    \begin{align*}
        \psi(ab) &= (ab)^2 \\ 
                 &= a^2b^2 \\ 
                 &= \psi(a)\psi(b) \\ 
        \psi(a+b)&= (a+b)^2 \\ 
                 &= a^2 + 2ab + b^2 \\ 
                 &\equiv a^2 + b^2 \\ 
                 &= \psi(a) + \psi(b) \\
        \psi(1)  &= 1^2 \\  &= 1.
    \end{align*}
    Let $\theta : \mathbb F_8 \to K$ be a field isomorphism, which 
    necessarily exists.
    Then $\sigma = \theta \circ \psi \circ \theta^{-1}$ is then 
    an automorphism on $K$.

    Now, 
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma(\alpha) 
        &= \alpha^2 \\ 
        \sigma^2(\alpha) \\ 
        &= \sigma(\alpha^2) \\ 
        &= \alpha^4 \\ 
        &= \alpha^2 + \alpha \\ 
        \sigma^3(\alpha) 
        &= \sigma(\alpha^4) \\ 
        &= \alpha^8 \\ 
        &= \alpha \cdot 1 \\ 
        &= \alpha. 
    \end{align*}
    Then $\operatorname{ord}(\sigma) = 3$. 

    For $\mathbb F_{q^n} / \mathbb F_q$ we have 
    that the extension is Galois with 
    $\operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb Q) = \mathbb Z/n\mathbb Z$ 
    generated by the Frobenious automorphism 
    $a \mapsto a^n$, and in fact 
    \[
        \langle \sigma \rangle = 
        \operatorname{Gal}(K/\mathbb F_2), 
    \]
    as we can see that $\sigma$ fixes $\{0,1\}$ and 
    $f(\sigma(\alpha)) = \sigma(f(\alpha)) = 0$. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        K = \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2, \sqrt 7).
    \]
    Prove that 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q] = 4. 
    \]
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    We will first show that $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$ is the 
    splitting field of $x^2 - 2$ over 
    $\mathbb Q$. 

    Let $L_{\sqrt 2}$ be the splitting field of $x^2 - 2$ over 
    $\mathbb Q$. Then 
    \[
        L_{\sqrt 2} \subseteq K
    \]
    since $K$ contains the roots $\pm \sqrt 2 \in L$ of $x^2 - 2$.

    Now since $\pm \sqrt 2 \in L_{\sqrt 2}$ and 
    $\mathbb Q\subseteq L_{\sqrt 2}$ we have that 
    $a + b\sqrt 2 \in L_{\sqrt 2}$ for any 
    $a,b \in \mathbb Q$ since $L_{\sqrt 2}$ is a field 
    and must therefore be closed under addition and multiplication 
    in a way which allows this. Therefeore 
    \[
        K \subseteq L_{\sqrt 2}. 
    \]

    For $\sqrt 2$, we have that $x^2 - 2$ is the minimal 
    polynomial and thus irreducible (can be shown with using 
    Eistenstein's criterion). Hence 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2) : \mathbb Q] = \deg(x^2 - 2) = 2. 
    \]

    We proceed now by showing that $\sqrt 2 \not \in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$. 
    Sppose $\sqrt 7 \in \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$. Then 
    \begin{align*}
        \sqrt 7 &= a + b\sqrt 2 \\ 
        7 &= a^2 + 2ab\sqrt2 + 2b^2. 
    \end{align*}
    We cannot have $b = 0$ since then 
    \[
        7 = a^2, 
    \]
    but $7$ is not the square of any 
    element $\in \mathbb Q$. 
    If $a = 0$, then 
    \[
        7 = 2b^2 
    \]
    and
