    \[
        \frac{7}{2} = b^2,
    \]
    but once again $7/2$ is not the 
    square of any element $\in\mathbb Q$.
    Otherwise if both are non-zero then 
    \[
        \frac{7 - a^2 - 2b^2}{2ab} = \sqrt 2
    \]
    meaning $\sqrt 2 \in \mathbb Q$ which is 
    not the case. Therefore $\sqrt 7 \not \in 
    \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$, so $[K:\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)] \neq 1$. 
    
    We use a similar argument as before that the minimal 
    polynomial of $\sqrt 7$ over $\mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)$ is 
    $x^2 - 7$ and therefore 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q(\sqrt 2)] = 2. 
    \]
    Using the tower law we get 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q] = 2 \cdot 2 = 4. 
    \]
\end{proof}
\begin{prob}
    Let  
    \[
        f(x) = x^3 - 5 \in \mathbb Q[x], 
    \]
    let 
    \[
        \alpha = \sqrt[3]5, 
    \]
    and let 
    \[
        \omega = e^{2i\pi/3}. 
    \]
    For the splitting field of $f$ over 
    $\mathbb Q$, compute it's degree over 
    $\mathbb Q$ and determine 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(f/\mathbb Q).
    \]
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    The roots of $f$ over $\mathbb C$ are 
    \[
        \alpha, \omega \alpha, \omega^2 \alpha. 
    \]
    The splitting field is 
    $\mathbb Q(\alpha, \omega\alpha)$.

    Then $f$ is still the minimal polynomial of 
    $\alpha$ over $\mathbb Q$ so 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha) : \mathbb Q] = 3. 
    \]

    We remark that $\omega \not\in \mathbb Q(\alpha)$ since 
    $\omega \in \mathbb C \supset \mathbb R \supset \mathbb Q(\alpha)$. 

    Now for $\omega$, the cube root of unity we compute 
    \[
        \prod_{d|n} (x^d - 1)^{\mu(n/d)}  
    \]
    which since $n = 3$ is prime simply gives 
    \[
        \sum_{n=0}^2 x^n = x^2 + x^1 + 1 
    \]
    as the minimal polynomial. 
    Therefore 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha, \omega) : \mathbb Q(\alpha)]
        = 2. 
    \]
    Using the tower law we get 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha,\omega): \mathbb Q] = 6. 
    \]
    The Galois group of a splitting field of 
    a degree $n$-polynomial is a subgroup of 
    $S_n$. The extension $f/\mathbb Q$ is Galois since 
    it is algebraic, normal (since all splitting fields are) and 
    sperable (since every extension of a characteristic $0$ field 
    which is algebraic is separable). 
    Then 
    \[
        [\mathbb Q(\alpha, \omega) : \mathbb Q] = 
        \operatorname{Gal}(f/\mathbb Q)
    \]
    so the Galois group is a subgroup of $S_3$ with 
    $6$ elements hence it is $S_3$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Suppose $E/\mathbb Q$ is a finite Galois extension and 
    \[
        \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q) \cong S_4.
    \]
    Prove that there exists an intermediate field $K$ with 
    \[
        [K : \mathbb Q] = 6. 
    \]
    Then decide whether $K$ is necessarily Galois 
    for your construction. 
\end{prob}
\begin{proof}
    For a subgroup $H \leq G := \operatorname{Gal}(E/\mathbb Q)$  
    we have that $[E:E^H] = |H|$. 
    We have $H:= \langle (1 \ 2 \ 3 \ 4) \leq S_4$ with 
    order $4 = 24/6$. Then since $E/\mathbb Q$ is 
    Galois we have
    \[
        [E : \mathbb Q] = |G| = 24. 
    \]
    Then 
    \[
        [E:E^{H}] = |H| = 4. 
    \]
    Then, by the tower law we have 
    \[
        [E^{H}:\mathbb Q] 
        = \frac{[E:\mathbb Q]}{[E:E^H]} = \frac{24}{4} = 6.
    \]
    Lastly $E^H/\mathbb Q$ is Galois if and only if 
    $H \trianglelefteq G$, which it is not. 
\end{proof}

\begin{prob}
    Let 
    \[
        G = \mathbb Z_{12} \times \mathbb Z_8
    \]
    and let 
    \[
        H = \langle (4,2) \rangle.
    \]
    Determine $G/H$ up to isomorphism.
\end{prob}

\begin{proof}
Let $G = \mathbb{Z}_{12} \times \mathbb{Z}_8$ and $H = \langle (4,2) \rangle$. To determine the structure of the quotient group $G/H$ using the First Isomorphism Theorem, we seek a surjective group homomorphism $\phi: \mathbb{Z}_{12} \times \mathbb{Z}_8 \to \mathbb{Z}_4 \times \mathbb{Z}_2$ such that $\ker(\phi) = H$.

First, let us analyze the cyclic subgroup $H$. By computing successive multiples of the generator $(4,2)$ in $G$, we find:
\begin{align*}
1 \cdot (4,2) &= (4,2) \\
2 \cdot (4,2) &= (8,4) \\
3 \cdot (4,2) &= (0,6) \\
4 \cdot (4,2) &= (4,0) \\
5 \cdot (4,2) &= (8,2) \\
6 \cdot (4,2) &= (0,4) \\
7 \cdot (4,2) &= (4,6) \\
8 \cdot (4,2) &= (8,0) \\
9 \cdot (4,2) &= (0,2) \\
10 \cdot (4,2) &= (4,4) \\
11 \cdot (4,2) &= (8,6) \\
12 \cdot (4,2) &= (0,0)
\end{align*}
Thus, $H$ is a subgroup of order $12$. By Lagrange's Theorem, the order of the quotient group must be:
\[
|G/H| = \frac{|G|}{|H|} = \frac{12 \times 8}{12} = 8
\]

We define a map $\phi: \mathbb{Z}_{12} \times \mathbb{Z}_8 \to \mathbb{Z}_4 \times \mathbb{Z}_2$ by:
\[
\phi(x, y) = (x - 2y \pmod{4}, \, y \pmod{2})
\]
We must check that $\phi$ satisfies the required properties:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Well-defined and Homomorphism:}
    The map is well-defined because if $x \equiv x' \pmod{12}$, then $x \equiv x' \pmod{4}$, and if $y \equiv y' \pmod{8}$, then $y \equiv y' \pmod{4}$ and $y \equiv y' \pmod{2}$. Furthermore, because reduction modulo $n$ and linear combinations are additive operations, it easily follows that for any $(x_1, y_1), (x_2, y_2) \in G$:
    \[
    \phi((x_1, y_1) + (x_2, y_2)) = \phi(x_1+x_2, y_1+y_2) = \phi(x_1, y_1) + \phi(x_2, y_2)
    \]
    Thus, $\phi$ is a group homomorphism.

    \item \textbf{Surjectivity:}
    Let $(a, b) \in \mathbb{Z}_4 \times \mathbb{Z}_2$. We can find a preimage $(x, y) \in G$ by setting $y = b \in \{0, 1\}$. Then we need $x - 2b \equiv a \pmod{4}$, which means we can choose $x = a + 2b \pmod{4}$. Since any value modulo $4$ can be trivially lifted to a valid residue modulo $12$, a preimage always exists, proving $\phi$ is surjective.

    \item \textbf{Kernel Verification:}
    By definition, $\ker(\phi) = \{ (x,y) \in G \mid \phi(x,y) = (0,0) \}$. This yields the system of congruences:
    \begin{align*}
    y &\equiv 0 \pmod{2} \implies y \in \{0, 2, 4, 6\} \\
    x - 2y &\equiv 0 \pmod{4} \implies x \equiv 2y \pmod{4}
    \end{align*}
    We check which elements $(x, y) \in \mathbb{Z}_{12} \times \mathbb{Z}_8$ satisfy this:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item If $y = 0$, then $x \equiv 0 \pmod{4} \implies x \in \{0, 4, 8\}$. This yields $(0,0), (4,0), (8,0)$.
        \item If $y = 2$, then $x \equiv 4 \equiv 0 \pmod{4} \implies x \in \{0, 4, 8\}$. This yields $(0,2), (4,2), (8,2)$.
        \item If $y = 4$, then $x \equiv 8 \equiv 0 \pmod{4} \implies x \in \{0, 4, 8\}$. This yields $(0,4), (4,4), (8,4)$.
        \item If $y = 6$, then $x \equiv 12 \equiv 0 \pmod{4} \implies x \in \{0, 4, 8\}$. This yields $(0,6), (4,6), (8,6)$.
    \end{itemize}
    Comparing this list of $12$ elements to our explicit generation of $H$, we see they are completely identical. Thus, $\ker(\phi) = H$.
\end{enumerate}

By the First Isomorphism Theorem, $G/\ker(\phi) \cong \text{Im}(\phi)$. Substituting our found structures yields:
\[
G/H \cong \mathbb{Z}_4 \times \mathbb{Z}_2
\]
\end{proof}
